RATING: 6
I really like this card mechanism and it makes an otherwise fairly standard and bland game into something worth playing. Whether it is sufficient to make the game a keeper in the long term is maybe doubtful because despite the fact that there are dozens and dozens of buildings available (which should create some variety) there are better resource conversion games out there (not least all of Rosenberg’s other games). The one thing that may just redeem it is the length of time it takes to play. If you can avoid any analysis paralysis (and there is plenty of scope for it). The game could even get by as a filler – there are only 4 rounds in total and they can play really quickly – particularly with only 2.

RATING: 6
Two things stand out about Glass Road. It's fundamentally a parsing problem, and chaotic resource management. First, what I really like about Glass Road is there is an element of psychology in terms of understanding what the other player(s) are after. The second thing I like about it is that the resources automatically convert, which really plays in when cards come out of your hand out of order courtesy of someone else playing a role. It feels interactive (because there are ways to gift resources to others), without being a direct conflict, and this plays in better at 3 and 4 (Supplier in particular becomes a weapon at 4p). In terms of focus. At 2p, it's a nice duel with extra control, while at 4p, you trade control of the central building board for more building churn (and thus, more options for building a chain). 

One thing that seems to bother us a bit (and really would if the game ran much longer) is you rarely see more than 1/3rd of the overall building pool in any given game, and combined with the fact that you'll see buildings whip past on other people's turns requires constant vigilance to the parsing problem. It runs a significant risk of being tactical or luck based without significant plays to get a handle on when risks can be taken or avoided (given the churn of buildings and knowing how fluid your chain can be). Understanding the cadence of card value across the game (especially Feudal Lord, Builder, and Supplier) is clutch.

There are some chokepoints (one good, one bad); first, you're going to have to pick all your cards each round which takes planning on the magnitude rarely seen (think; Lewis & Clark where you setup camp and then immediately start ordering your next 4 or 5 turns in general). Its a similar calculation to Millennium Blades in that you have 15-20 options and you're cutting them in half or so. The second one is related to the parsing problem that surrounds building/feudal lord; at what point does this just become unmanageable to say "i'm going to accumulate end game scoring points in ways X/Y/Z" vs something else? Playing with people who have AP is crushing due to these two choke points, especially since the game is played in lock step...

Some of my best games have been at 4p, and realistically, it's what I think I shoot for. Possibly my third favorite Uwe game behind O&L and Bohnanza.

RATING: 7
A quick, light typically Rosenberg themed game of acquiring resources and building, with some unique mechanisms, and some wacky double-guessing gameplay.

The game plays from 2-4 players, and is played quickly over 4 short rounds.  Each round, you select 5 cards from a choice of 15 cards (each player has the same set of 15 different character cards which provide abilities), and choose 1 to play- this is where the craziness can occur: when another player has the card that you chose to play in their hand, they get to "tag along" getting half of the action and you only get half of the action, good for them, bad for you, so there is a significant component of guessing what your opponent will do.  The cards are predominantly used to acquire resources and build buildings.  The resource collection is unique and elegant and somewhat thematic, involving two separate wheels/dials, one for producing glass and one for producing brick- when you acquire resources they can become consumed in the production of the high end goods (glass, brick) and so managing these resources can be subtle and tricky- you really need to play a game to understand.  

Buildings are the means of getting points (and this is a LOW scoring game) and they come in three flavours: 1) production buildings- allow you to convert resources into different types, 2) one time buildings- provide an immediate effect/boost, 3) end of game scoring buildings.  There is a very large number of these buildings, which keeps the game fresh/variable.  It should be noted that each player has their own personal (identical) player board that has some space for buildings, but which also has land types (forest, brush, claypits, lakes) than need to be cleared/managed, somewhat similar to Ora et Labora.

Definitely a very light game, but since it plays so quick, the randomness that can occur is easily tolerated.  Appears to play quite well at all numbers.

Own the [boardgame=167567]Oktoberfest[/boardgame], and the [boardgame=189653]Die Glasstrasse: Advent Calendar[/boardgame] promo tiles as well as the [boardgame=190602]Harlekin[/boardgame] promo card.

Very refreshing game from Mr. Rosenberg. After Agricola and Le Havre his games have been big disappointments to me. They all failed in delivering the kind of elation you get from games with original ideas.

At first, Glass Road is daunting since the role selection and the resource mechanics are very untypical to a game of resource management. I really hope this proves to be on par with the two aforementioned masterpieces.

Rating from 6 -> 7
The more you play the better the game feels. I really like the action selection mechanic giving you hard time in analysing your opponents. What are the actions they would be trying to leech from you, and vice versa? You have to appreciate how dense and tense Glass Road is. Every choice you make is a difficult one. And all this is achieved without an overwhelming the amount of information.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6
Wow!

I'd say this offers almost as interesting of a decision-making space as some of the 'larger' Rosenberg games but at substantially less of a time investment.  

I really like the card mechanic.  In short, players select five cards from a pool of 15 and then get to play three--if a card is played that is unique (other players didn't choose it), the player playing it gets to take both actions on the card; if a card is played that is non-unique, all players that have that card get to take one of the two actions on the card.  There's some other caveats around how that mechanic works, but it provides for a really interesting planning phase at the beginning of each round, where you are trying to choose some cards that are unique (you don't expect other players to play them, so hope to achieve the double action available on the card) and some cards that are non-unique (you hope other players will choose them so you can get a 'bonus' single action off the card plus deny your opponent the double action).  

With only four rounds to play and effective card choice having the possibility to swing your number of actions anywhere from 3 (you play three cards, all of which the other players also chose, and they played no card matching your other two) to 8 (you play three cards, none of which the other players chose, granting you two actions per each of those three cards; plus you happen to hold two cards that were each selected by at least one other player, granting you one bonus action per each of those two cards), you really have to try to understand not only your own strategy, but also your opponents' strategies so you can more effectively guess what they will and will not select.

RATING: 7
An interesting game.  Plays with Mr. Rosenberg's produce/consume engine in a different manner.  The wheels drive all, yet they merely reflect all the stuff happening via cards played and the individual player boards.

I want to like this one more but I don't.  Going early in the turn order is worse.  Having players match your lead is actually painful unless you reciprocate.  In our 3-player game, we had rounds where one person had 7 actions, another (me) had 6 actions, and the third had 4 actions.  Guess who won?  The guy who got to take more actions.  Guess who came in second?  Me, with the second-most actions.  This disparity can be painful if not crippling and it's endemic to the game.

I have heard that this plays better with 2 players.  I wouldn't mind trying it out and seeing if that's preferable to 3 or 4 players.

RATING: 8
1st play - I was a little lost on what to do or how to approach this game.  So I picked a building and started on my way.  My wife decided she understood what to do right away and drafted off my actions like crazy. I think I only took her actions twice, she hit me 1-2 times EVERY round, and she won 20 to my 15.5.  The suspense of revealing your action unsure whether you'll be able to double up or not is great and the playtime is short.

2nd play - I approached this time with a plan, ready to be more efficient with my moves. I found building synergy and got a decent engine going. Unfortunately my wife did the same and beat me 23-22.  Honestly I went into this game thinking I could play my own board without looking at hers. She looked at mine and drafted off of me like crazy. In one round I went first, she drafted me twice and emptied her hand before me. 

This game improves on the Witch's brew mechanic in that it's not all or nothing, but it's still very powerful.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A fairly fast-playing game with limited downtime between turns, interesting decisions, and a lot of variety in the buildings. It is a very "puzzle-y" game that can at times be frustrating, but makes for a great, fun challenge.

The card play allows for a good deal of interaction. In a two-player game, you each select five cards (out of fifteen available) before the round starts. You take turns playing a card. If you are the only person with that card in hand when you play it, you get to do both actions listed on the card. If your opponent has the same card in hand when you play yours, they are also able to play it on your turn, and each of you only gets to pick one of the two available actions. The round ends when one person has no more cards left. So, you really need to pay careful attention to your opponent. You are trying to strike a balance between choosing cards you don't think he/she will choose, so you can get the maximum benefit from the card, and choosing cards you think he/she will play, so that you can piggyback on those actions and also keep the person from gaining both of the card's benefits. 

I love how the resource wheels are implemented. They are a big part of the "puzzle" of the game. Basically, you have two wheels: one that simulates your glass production, and one for your brick production. You start the game with some resources, and as you acquire them and move them along your wheel, the wheel will turn forward when you have enough resources to produce a batch of glass or brick. You cannot prevent this from happening. Thematically, I see it as you are overseeing both of these businesses and your land, and when you gather resources to be delivered to the glass or brick producers, and have paid them with food, they then go to work right away. So that might not seem like a big deal--after all, you want to make glass and brick. However, you will need the resources throughout the game to build buildings, pay for certain specialists to provide you with actions (the cards), or to convert into other resources or points using building powers. So, when the wheel turns to simulate production, your 1 clay, 1 charcoal, and 1 food will suddenly turn into brick, and you will have zero of each good. You might have needed that one food to play the builder specialist card. You can only produce and store three glass and three brick at any one time, which means you might want to spend them to build buildings and keep your production lines flowing. Anyway, it is quite a puzzle.

There are three different types of buildings--ones that give you production powers you can use whenever you would like once you've built them, ones that grant a one-time bonus, and ones that give you additional ways to score points at the end of the game. You are building these buildings on your land, which at the beginning of the game is comprised of forests, ponds, sand pits, and groves. So your land is a puzzle all its own. You will need to clear land to make room for buildings, and also adapt your landscape to maximize the powers of the buildings you build. You might get points for each pond adjacent to the building, or for each connected sand pit you have on your land. Some buildings allow you to instantly add landscape to your land once built, while others will allow you to destroy certain landscape to gain resources instead. So the buildings you choose to build often force you to manipulate your land in a certain way.

All of our plays have been around 45 minutes with two players.

RATING: 7.5
It's good. It has simple mechanisms. It's easy to teach. And yet, it has solid gameplay.

I've only played solo and with four players. With four players and a large table, I couldn't really see what anyone was doing, and I didn't really have a good idea what they would go for, so the whole "pick X number of cards that will match and Y number that won't match your opponents selection" was largely thrown out of the window. I suspect that the game will actually be a more social experience with two players, than it is with four, since you'll be able to really pay attention to what they're working on. And without thinking about it at all, with four players, I'd say that I got an average of 4 cards played each round. It would probably be hard to do significantly better than that.

Really, the only part the other players had for me was in the choice of order of playing my cards, since cards are "in danger" while they are in your hand. So you want to play things in priority order, rather than in optimal order.

Ultimately, not a big issue since the game is still good and fairly short (even including the rules explanation). But expect to have it be multiplayer solitaire unless you're playing with two.

My only other thought on the game is one thing I noticed in the rules which is that the first player token simply rotates in 2 and 4 player modes. In a 3 player game, on the 4th round the player who is last in scoring gets to go first. That's a bit... Well, I feel like there had to be a better solution than that. This seems like it would be annoying no matter what, because everyone has to compute their score one round before having to compute their score AGAIN, for the end of the game. And then, if the scores were anything like close to each other at the end of the third round, it's entirely conceivable that the player who was furthest in back might win the game due to getting the first player bonus on hitting the market.

Personally, the stop-to-score element bugs me more, but I'm sure there are lots of people who would be annoyed at having the single-person-target catch-up mechanism that might overshoot.

It would have been better if Uwe had used a bidding mechanism or told the first player to lose two resources and the second player to lose one resource, at the start the fourth round, or something. Or maybe put out three tiles and let the players each pick one in reverse turn order.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A light and fun game, especially with two. It offers the crunchy decisions of a longer resource euro, but plays in half the time. 

The variable nature of the buildings, especially the end of scoring buildings, change things up just enough to make each play feel different. That said, the variability has been overstated a bit. Many of the buildings are merely slight variations on a theme (gather 4 sand tiles for 4 VP, gather 4 lake tiles for 4 VP, etc). 

Criticisms related solely to initial plays -- forcing new players to preprogram the first 5 actions in the game can be a bit overwhelming for some. It takes about half a play to really become familiar with the cards on offer too. Still, saying the game offer room for growth and familiarity is never really a negative.

RATING: 7
Rosenberg's designs have not been my cup of English Breakfast since....oh....Bohnanza.  Hence Glass Road was something of a pleasant surprise.  

As so often before, Glass Road is about resource harvesting and conversion, this time into buildings that score victory points.  It even has individual player boards, to which I am practically allergic.  

What Glass Road does right, in my view, is to keep the resource business simple.  The two dials help with that, as well as surprising the unwary by automatically converting basic resources into brick and glass.  I also like the Witch's Brew card play whereby you can reduce the effectiveness of other players' actions and leech off them.  Plus, Glass Road zips along at  good pace and doesn't outstay its welcome.

Production quality is good.  The artwork is unexciting but quite pleasing to the eye.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Certainly the most unique of Uwe Rosenberg's 'big games'. It features two mechanisms that aren't commonly found elsewhere. First is the brick and glass resource wheels and their 'auto-generation' (when you get at least one of the input goods, you MUST produce one manufactured good, to a limit for three manufactured goods). It provides an interesting extra wrinkle in the resource producing puzzle.

The other mechanism is the action selection system, which (as acknowledged in the rules) is also found, in a modified form, in Broom Service. Each player has a deck of 15 cards, each with 2 actions, one on the top and one on the bottom. For each round chooses 5 cards and sets aside the rest. Then for each turn (there are 3 per round) player choose one card to play for that turn. Starting with the round's start player, they reveal their selected card and ask if anybody else has the card in their hand. If nobody else has it, they can do both actions; but if somebody has, then the current player does only one action, and the following player takes another action. Note that each player can follow only two times each round

I think that, if you internalize the system, there is great depth for strategic (in the card choices in each round) and tactical (in making card choices in each turn) planning. But the number and complexity of the role cards makes it hard to learn and especially internalized, so it can feel like you are just randomly choosing cards.

As for the rest, it's typical euro game tropes, if individual player board, engine building and resource conversion. Sometimes I like these tropes and sometimes I don't; with this game I like it because the game is quite concise in it length and components, which I appreciate.

I've also been pleasantly surprise how well it played with two players. Here there is no simultaneous turn selection; instead, each player will choose cards (and possibly follow) until one player runs out of cards. I think since there is only one opponent to consider, it makes it easier to try and outthink your opponent.

RATING: N/A
05.01.03.02
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Glass Road is one of the two big box games designed by Uwe Rosenberg in 2013, and it surely is the most peculiar of the two: while it can seem another game in the camp of the resource converters Uwe is a master at, it is quite short and has at its core a couple of interesting and different enough mechanics to stand out.

First mechanic is more a "technological" innovation: the two wheels used for managing resources are quite clever and very functional, saving many minutes of playing time.

On the other hand, the role selection and interaction is very interesting and interactive, and it's the true engine of the game.

A couple of things keep Glass Road from being a super-duper game: it's thematically very dry and disconnected, and it has a banned building (Roofing Company) that slipped through playtesting.

Overall is a very good game, and the initial rating (7.5) could realistically go up if it plays well with two.

RATING: 8
A good role selection, economic, puzzle game that can be a brain-burner for new players.  Essentially, it's a game about converting resources into buildings or other resources so you can earn victory points.  A central feature and tricky part of the game is that you secretly pick 5 roles and then gradually play 3 of them per round as actions, and you hope to get bonus actions by matching your 4th or 5th cards with roles that opponents choose to play as one of their 3 actions.  Another trick is in figuring out how to stay out of your own way because when you produce a rare resource, your goods wheel turns and you consume/destroy your basic resources.  Most buildings require both rare and basic resources so, if your not careful, you may end up consuming your entire supply of a basic resource required to make that building this round.

UPDATE #1: I still really enjoy the challenge of this game, but I worry that it's imbalanced toward the person who matches card choices with opponents and gets extra actions.  The final scores are often very low (~20-30) and close together, thus 1/2 points and extra actions are a big deal.

UPDATE #2: Perhaps the game is not imbalanced but just heavily rewards players who are paying close attention to what other players are doing and what they need/want for their success (rather than playing in isolation as multi-player solitaire and hoping that you match enough cards to maximize your number of actions in this short #turns game).  Being attentive to game situations of other players can be difficult in my regular group due to distractions (noise, music, lighting, chatting, and late arrivals) and the distance between player boards/wheels relative to the small iconography for building costs, conversion abilities on tiles, etc.  The bottom line is that I think the game shines when played in an intimate setting with a small table and focused players, and in the absence of this setting the game feels luck-heavy, doesn't gel or chain nicely, and is not as much fun.

RATING: 8
10x10 2014. I love Uwe Rosenberg games, and Ora et Labora in particular.  And this game reminds me of Ora in several ways- the goods on the wheels (although it's used pretty differently here than in Ora) and building your own little town.  So I wanted this to be Ora light and I think this is about as close as any game is going to come to that experience in a short (approximately 1 hr or faster for 2p) time frame.  The card play is interesting and the mechanic where you can play cards when others play them, or if you play them and no one else has them you get a bigger benefit is neat.  Also the mechanic of the glass works and brick works advancing the wheel- running automatically basically is neat.  It's a really slick way to use one of a bunch of stuff to make one really good thing.  Although forcing them to run whenever possible does feel a little wonky sometimes since it will occasionally mess up your plans.  The game ends a little quickly but you can play an extra round, as it says in the rule book, for a longer game.

RATING: 7
So happy I finally decided to get a copy.  It's a Rosenberg harvest game, but it's a quick one and it's also got a very nice Witches' Brew mechanic where players choose a subset of cards to determine their actions and then get bonus actions for outguessing what actions their opponents are going to choose.  It takes his normal harvest games and makes them more interactive.  A fairly quick playtime (~1 hour) makes this even better.

RATING: 8.5
I am loving this new trend of card-driven, hand management Euros (Rococo, Lewis & Clark, Concordia, City of Iron): it feels like worker placement but adds the more interactive feeling of trying to predict and benefit from your opponents' actions. 

Glass Road stands out by adding in the spatial and production elements of Rosenberg's earlier designs (Ora & Labora), not to mention the coolest, most ingenious resource tracker I've ever seen in a board game: the first time you make brick or glass, you will "ooh" and "aah" at the magical wheel. I also like that for once you don't have to feed your stupid family.

RATING: 6
A neat little game of resource management through card play and buying of buildings with all sorts of special abilities that can act in various ways.

On one hand, it all works well, but on the other hand, I feel like it's a game that really didn't do much to innovate, which left me a little unexcited.  None the less, it's fun to juggle around the resources and figure out how to best navigate our desired path, just nothing mind blowing that would make me choose it over many other games.

The one neat aspect is the card play.  Every round, you choose 5 cards to play.  When someone plays a card and you have it in your hand, you must follow them, allowing each of you to take a weak version of that action.  However, if no one follows, the player that played the card gets a much stronger version of the action.  Thus, you're always looking at what you think other players might do, and trying to do what's best for you as well as what you think other players may not be interested in.

Overall, there's a neat little twist in the card play, but the rest is your very standard resource manipulation.  Good to check out, but for me, it's nothing excitingly new although it is fine to play.

RATING: 8
Uwe has thing where he designs games that revolve around semi-realistically manipulating real world ingredients and I just enjoy that.

Unlike most of his games of this sort, the game isn't about blocking, but rather about leeching. And leeching properly is critical, since the most actions you can get in a turn is 8, but the lowest is 3. There is a big difference between 8 and 3.

I also appreciate that you won't see all the buildings every game, and I felt like I needed to try and build a strategy around the buildings I bought at the beginning of the game.

RATING: 3
Like Rosenberg's Ora, this is an opaque solitaire puzzle game of resource manipulation and conversion. The amount of permutations generated by the large number of resources and actions, and their interactions with each other and with the seemingly endless amount of buildings is just absurd. 

There really aren't the guiding principles of something like Chess to pare down the decision trees and the scope of the information overload either. It amounts to flailing around and stabbing in the dark. 

The cardplay seems like it would be an interesting source of interaction, but it isn't. Since you barely have what you could even plausibly call a tenuous handle on your own actions, how can you possibly make an educated guess as to someone else's intentions? And then as people acquire VP buidlings, it becomes obvious which action cards they have to select every time to acquire VPs, so there really is no decision there either. There isn't much interesting middle ground.

I play with people who like Rosenberg's games, so I have to keep playing them. And I do well against those people (all strong players), so I don't feel like I'm missing anything. I just think the emperor is buck naked and some people get a thrill out of fondling his balls.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love Uwe’s games - Agricola being my favorite game of all time. This one is much lighter than many of his other “big” games, but it’s actually quite good, especially considering how short it is. Actually, the first time I played it I thought we played it wrong (which we did not) because it was over in like 30 minutes. This obviously also means that you don’t really get to do a whole lot in the game, but it works just fine as a short, light game.

The gears and how you produce resources is a neat mechanic just like the three types of buildings (instant ressources, conversion buildings and end game buildings). But there is no doubt that the greatest feature of Glass Road is the card mechanic itself! GR rewards you for choosing cards which the other players are going to play and for playing cards yourself which they haven’t chosen… So you really need to evaluate the situation of each player and try to outthink them (which also creates some subtle player interaction)! Because if you play a card nobody else has chosen, you get both of the actions on the cards. And if you can match one of their cards, you both get to do just one of the actions. This of course means that it’s crucial to pick the right cards. Some might think it’s random which player chooses which cards, but I highly disagree – it’s most certainly possible to estimate which cards other players are likely to play (if he doesn’t have any type of one resource he might play that, or if he has a lot of lakes he might play the card which gives him x number of sand or water for the number of lakes etc).

The downtime is minimal because the game is so fast and because you choose cards simultaneously. I’ve tried it with 2,3 and 4, and while 2 player works a little differently, all player counts work well. Some minor niggles are that it can be a little hard to choose cards and which resources you need to produce glass and bricks. And when you have one of each resource, the wheel turns which might not be what you wanted, but it can happen if another player plays a card. You can also get a little screwed if another player plays (especially) a building card before you wanted to because you don’t have the resources at that time, or you thought you would get both actions etc, but that’s pretty cool. 

The buildings themselves aren’t that interesting. Those that convert aren’t used too often I find as they don’t provide any points, and as the game is over so quickly they rarely get used a lot. Those that grant an instant bonus, well, they just provide you with an immediate bonus and then they’re worth nothing afterwards (except for the points of course). The end game buildings are very important and help you shape your strategy, so it’s a pain in the ass when another player grabs the building you had your eyes on! I think it was a little weird that there were so few buildings available and that so rather few new buildings came out each round. But all in all GR is a good, although not great, game.

RATING: 7
Rosenberg tends to be more "miss" than "hit" for me. I really like Ora et Labora, my few plays of Le Havre were okay, I'll play Caverna if someone wants, and I'll avoid Agricola.

So, Glass Road was a pleasant surprise. My buddy described it as "Ora lite" and that surprisingly seems about right. You've got the dials for semi-automated resource management, buildings and terrain placed on a grid where placement and adjacency can matter, but actions are chosen via card play and trying to guess what other players will choose so that you can piggyback on actions. All this happens in four quick rounds.

It's not a light game, but it's certainly very approachable. I'd gladly play this again.

This game is delightful. It plays quickly, so much variety in the number of buildings, and tough decisions on what 5 workers to hopefully use each turn. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Rating and comments for the solo game. This is a hard solo experience, with a reasonable learning curve. It is not a straightforward solo, like Agricola, because you have to understand how various elements intertwine: the production wheels, the distribution of actions on the cards, the currency exchange rate, the hand size limitations and more. There are plenty of paths to explore, and a lot of control that can be exercised regardless of the luck of the tile draw. One weak point is that your score does depend on the seeding of the board, so to improve your top score, you have to be skilled but also lucky to get a good draw. A very enjoyable, quick to setup, small footprint game that is unlike anything else in my collection. Overall, a great solo experience and worth the admission ticket alone.

RATING: 6
First play. Didn't quite live up to my expectations, which were perhaps a bit too high. There are so many tiles to build (the whole point of the game), and you don't see them until they come out, and they do so many different things, that it feels like you don't really even know what game you're playing until it unfolds in front of you. For some reason I expected a more in depth game. With multiple resource wheels and tons of different resources and zillions of ways to transmute them. There's a hefty dose of randomness and chaos. I look forward to playing more, but I may have to rejigger my expectations for what kind of game this is.

RATING: 6
A friendly game of simple resource conversion in the spirit of Le Havre and Ora & Labora, but then over and done in under an hour. Perfect for fraidy cats and dumbos like me who have trouble playing the Big Games because of not being able to easily visualise the conversion chains. Glass Road quickly offers a lot of resources, doesn't overstay its welcome with tons of game rounds, doesn't ask difficult questions of you. There are circumstances in which I want to be asked these questions, but not with resource conversion and mild snowballing. 

What struck me most during my first game is that the fear I had of undesired clashes in the card play has been shrewdly manipulated by Uwe in that you actually [i]want[/i] some clashes and some uniqueness: it's the only way in which you get to play more than 3 out your 5 chosen characters. Thank you for that very simple and effective twist to make what would have otherwise been a painful process, Uwe. As soon as that cloud evaporated, I found myself even enjoying Glass Road... more than I thought I would, in any case. As I said, you shouldn't take this game all too seriously if you have the aforementioned Big Box games by Uwe for breakfast. I think a better yardstick is your appreciation of his lighter titles: Loyang and Merkator. Glass Road is like that: stuff distilled down to (a lot of) their essence. 

I think I can even see this game enter my collection at some point in the future. Not at full price point (sorry, Uwe) because in the end it is still resource conversion which is not a game mechanism I generally have much patience with. But still, there aren't many games which do what Glass Road does. And that is something which ought to be rewarded.

[b]Update[/b] Got the game sometime later (at reduced price point, it was on sale), and played it. It remains a friendly game most of the people I've introduced it to seem to like. Not great, not particularly good or special, but charming in its own sweet inoffensive way. It's a good game to bring to meetings which don't happen often. Increased the rating by 1.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is not a bad game by any means, but there were a couple of problems which kept it from hitting the table much at our house:

1. The game ends before it feels like it should. The rulebook includes a variant to add a round for a longer game, but even at this the game end seems abrupt - like how Agricola ends just as your farm is really starting to run smoothly, only worse. (We even tried with +2 rounds, which was better but the ending still left something to be desired.)

2. There are a bunch of tiles, all with small text, and it's tough for all players to see them from across the table. It's also hard to formulate an overall strategy while trying to read and comprehend the tiles.

I really like the resource dials. They are a clever mechanic, but in the end they weren't enough to prop up the game. Traded away.

RATING: 6

Experience: Really tough to wrap my brain around. The resource wheels seem opaque just for opaqueness's sake. I think I'm missing a piece of the theme here, but having two separate wheels, and forcing me to convert my resources into brick/glass, even at inopportune moments, just feels weird and difficult to understand.

On a first play, there was just too much information: too many cards to choose from; too many buildings. And it's not easy to see a production chain. At one point I wanted to clear a forest so that I could connect two ponds. To do that, I had to select cards to get the right resources to build a building that would allow me to clear forests, and then I had to select the cards to give me the resources to build the building that cared about ponds; and then of course I had to build the pond itself. That was basically half my game, just to get four points.

RATING: 7.5
After 3 plays; 1 play @ 2 players, 2 plays @ 1 player

A decent resource puzzle where you try to build up your town for points while managing a two production wheels and a half dozen resources. Players can try to anticipate and chain off the actions of another player to accomplish more in a round. This chaining action seems to screw the first player more since later players will be out of cards sooner. Since the game only lasts four rounds, it can be painful to be first. 

I actually enjoyed the game more solo. Without the action chaining, the game has more of a puzzle feel where you're trying to do the most with the tiles available and manage the resources in the best way. 

I would play again if someone wanted to play but not one that I'm interested in adding to my collection.

RATING: 7
Some people have objected to the Witch's Brew-like action mechanism, though I kind of dig it (I like Wie verhext, after all).  Some people have objected to the auto-cube converter track, though I found it fun and thematic.  My real problem with this game is actually something others would find a feature, which is that a handful of times during my two plays I was finally set to build a bonus building, the key to my strategy, only to have it taken right before I could.  In other games you are simply forced to adapt when this happens; to find a new strategy, or go to a plan B.  And while you still do that here, the bonus buildings are so random and possibly disparate that you really can't take advantage of your infrastructure the same way.  To me it makes what should be a great strategy game into an ok tactical game.  I still give it a bump for the fun mechanisms, however.

RATING: 5
The game pulls in two different directions - it wants to be a quick-playing light and frothy Euro but then poses quite fiddly and complex resource gathering, VP conversion calculations that builds AP into the heart of the game. However, if you just ditch the quick playing bit and go whole hog with it as a brain-burner, it then ladles a large dollop of randomness/luck into the game in the form of whether you second-guess the cards others have chosen and therefore get to play off other peoples cards or not. 

The theme is, as usual for these kinds of Euros, nowhere really. There is also such a wide variety of buildings that there is lots of peering at what's available and how that ties in with the choice of 15 cards, and I can't see this diminishing even with repeated plays.

The resource wheel mechanism is very clever and the game is wonderfully produced with lots of nice art and chunky bits of cardboard. These two factors contribute an extra 0.5 to my rating but underneath it's just another Euro and rather dull.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players are glass manufacturers in Bavaria.

Pleasant, fast playing game where players select five role cards, from a hand of fifteen, to play out a round.  There are four of these rounds in the game.  Players select a role and action it, but if other players have that role in their hand, they reveal it and leech an action off the phasing player.  Actions are typical spend stuff to gain stuff things - resources that are tracked on a pair of production wheels that manufacture brick and glass automatically when the resources are present.

Resources are tapped back into buildings that are constructed on a player's board.  These grant abilities and victory points at the end of the game.

This is a chewy, meaty one hour game that just ... isn't that much fun.  It's well designed, but the scoring and game play is so constrained and tight, having your actions leeched can mean the difference between a victory and a loss.  We got eight plays out of it - and surprisingly found the two player game better than the four player game.  

Worth a look but ultimately not for us.

RATING: 7
It's like a cross between Witch's Brew and Ora&Labor Junior.   The wheel mechanism is clever, as producing glass and brick costs you a different combination of other resources which will vary each time due to token placement when the wheel closes up the gaps.   There seems to be a wide variety of buildings to keep it varied.   It has some of the trademark Uwe oddities, and that's likely part of the appeal.  Plays pretty quickly, so that's a nice feature.   Doesn't completely light me on fire, but I don't mind playing it either.   Almost the textbook definition of a "middle of the road" 7 for me.

I think the main criticism that I have is that the game doesn't really have any sense of theme, and it definitely has no arc.  The buildings don't really make any sense and there isn't as much sense of having constructed something as there is in Agricola or Ora Et Labora.   I want a sense of achievement and this game doesn't have it.   It's more about eeking out half a point here or half a point there.

RATING: 7
Enjoyable game - 4 rounds of three to five moves each. Each round you select five cards, and if any of your opponents play the same card (roughly), you both get to do a half-action. So ideally you'll get 3 full actions, 2 half actions. Reminded me in terms of the limited moves and plethora of good choices of Edo. On top of that, there is a clever mechanism where resources are on two wheels. If all the resources are 1 or higher on the wheel... the wheel turns back to 0 and you get a premium resource worth VPs at the end (and required to get better buildings). Finally, you have a board to build on - but the spaces you build over can be exploited for VPs and resources - if you get the buildings for it, and can afford them.

RATING: 9
Glass Road is Uwe Rosenberg's latest resource conversion game. Le Havre led to Ora and Labora led to The Glass Road. If you enjoyed those games, particularly O&L, you will enjoy this. 

It is shorter than either of it's forefathers, playing in a realistically achievable 20 mins per person. There are just 4 rounds and between 3 and 8 actions are played per round. You are building buildings which do 'stuff' and give VPs. It has a neat card mechanism whereby you select 5 action cards from a 15 card action deck which you all have identical copies of. You play 3 of these cards in a round. Each card has 2 actions. But when you play a card, if anyone else has it in their hand, then you can only use one of the actions, and the other player gets to use the card 'for free' too. So you are not only trying to select the cards with actions you want to use, you also need to be mindful of what others are playing as you want to play cards yourself which they don't have, so you can get to use both actions. Meanwhile you want to hold in your hand cards other players will use so you can get 'free' actions whilst also halving your opponent's use of the actions on their card. 

So...lot's to think about and a lot of the time is spent determining just which 5 cards you will take in to the round with you. Oh, and it has a really clever resource wheel which is a thing of great wonder.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
W
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After 1 play: My wife and I played one two player game and we both liked it. The game was pretty quick to teach and learn, nothing too complicated with the buildings that come up and the rules come with a comprehensive building list with directions.

Glass Road is a different game from the big box games like Agricola or Le Havre. But I came away thinking it borrowed a bit from them and threw in some Bohnanza style player interaction. While there's no trading, you really need to pay attention to what other players might be planning on to take advantage of their cardplay. The 2 player game plays quite different than the 3 or 4 player game, which seem to be the better player count. But even the two player game offers a lot of opportunity to take advantage of each other.

Purchased for $65-25 discount, this game seems like a game well worth that price. Nice components, interesting decisions throughout all phases of the game, a good rulebook and that nice Lohausen art.

After 5 plays: Many of my games have been solo, this category is still pending. But good solo game!

After 10 plays:

RATING: 9
Love, love, love this game.  For me it's just the right amount of depth--it's a "light" game for Uwe Rosenberg, but mid-weight more generally. Although it's a bit of a "brain burner," it's a short game and really doesn't take much more than an hour to play, if all players are familiar with the game (and aren't too prone to AP).  

I think of it in the same category as Puerto Rico--the mechanics are quite different, but the games are similar in time, depth, ratio of skill to luck, and interactivity among the players.  

I absolutely love the resource wheel--it's a fun mechanic that always makes my head hurt a little bit--and it is unique among the games I've played.  There isn't a good one or two sentence way to explain it--but in sum:  when you generate one resource, you *may or may not* lose other resources.  But which resources you lose (and how many of each resource) depends on the location of the resources in relation to the dial and to each other. 

Glass Road also seems a lot more forgiving than say, Agricola.  An early mistake may set you back, but not insurmountably (and end game mistake though is another matter).

RATING: 8
First impression was thinking that I wouldn't like it. "Typical eurogame of get resources and then VPs blah blah blah." But it turns out to be quite interesting after all. The key is the role selection where it's about managing between choosing unique roles (maximising your actions for that role and denying other players a chance to play extra cards) and choosing roles that other players have chosen (so you can get extra actions). Not sure about replayability with the same players because groupthink and figuring out a player's pattern will make the game less challenging. But for now, pleasantly surprised and enjoying the game.

RATING: 5
[1 play] I'm not sure what I think about Glass Road, but it deserves another chance. I learned it under somewhat inauspicious circumstances: at a con, from people who were enthusiastic but had just recently learned the game themselves. Not a recipe for the best teaching. I was encouraged to not worry about the big picture but rather to just try to grasp the game as it unfolded. That worked OK for the logistics of what to do moment to moment, but at the end of the game I felt like I still didn't understand what I wanted to. I think this problem would not have existed with more thoughtful and coherent teaching. The jury is out on this till I have a chance to play it again. Speaking of historical info, though, I did not know any of the history this game is based in, and I thought it was fascinating. I should also note that this was pitched to me on the basis of the Witch's Brew-esque mechanic, which I thought was interestingly done.

RATING: 6
This might be the Uwe Rosenberg game for me. 

Well...my initial impression might be true, if only because the game is so short. You don't really accomplish much unless you focus on a strategy early. I don't suggest ever upgrading your core structures and be sure to accumulate the max sand, glass and brick (9 points). Scoring over 20 seems to be a challenge, but if you do, you probably won the game.

Optimally you want to match 2 of your opponents cards and have several exclusive (double effect) cards plays. If you're lucky to do this twice in the 4 rounds, the advantages are telling.

Glad to have played it, but it replaces nothing in my collection.

RATING: 8
Enjoyed this enough to play again immediately. Strongly advise beginners do begin with the beginners' tiles. Our first game used all the tiles, we both only managed 2 builds in the game! Second game with just the beginners tiles went much better, we built lots more and improved scores.

The wheel mechanism is brilliant, very clever and the Next Big Thing. But I also enjoyed the subtle inter-action of double-thinking your opponents' choices to glom more stuff from their plays and to de-glom them/get the double action bonus yourself. AND, I really liked the action of clearing forest both provide resources and roome to build, but removing ponds pits and groves did not need an action.

All together a sweet package, collect, convert, build to VPs all boiled down to a very tight quick game. So much potential for other designers to steal from.

I predict, Kennerspiel 2014.

RATING: 4
[b]Short playtime resource-conversion game in which players can piggyback extra actions if they guess what others' plans are. Too short to be fulfilling, I'd rather play something either more or less complex. After 4 plays, I don't need to play this ever again.

January 2016: With my 3rd play a few months ago, and my 4th play yesterday, I can say that the game made more sense to me, but that it just suffers in comparison to other games it reminds me of. Certainly not a bad game, but no spark in it to bring me back.

June 2014: The art turns me off a little bit, as it lacks the character I expect from Uwe Rosenberg games, but the gameplay was nice. You blind choose actions, hoping to piggyback for efficiency (not quite like any other game I've played),. The land-clearing, building, and (occassional) resource flipping is vaguely like Ora et Labora. The multiple paths to victory (largely through building is pure Rosenberg. A good light-middleweight with a short playtime. Yet still feels aimed at gamers to me: the blah artwork, and complex rondel management won't allow this to play well as a family game, I think. I suspect my rating will fall to a 7 after about 8 plays, but I hope I'm wrong.

Some similarities to Ora et Labora, but not the rondel (which is used entirely differently). The similarities are in removing terrain for resources, adjacent tile placement effects, and resource conversion.

RATING: 9
Glass Road plays like a lighter, shorter version of Le Havre or Ora et Labora but with almost all of the fun and more randomness.  It includes a significant bluffing element that can affect how productive each turn is, depending on how accurately I can predict the actions my opponents will take.  This is a plus for me but will no doubt be a minus for many people. I love the increased interaction. There are a ton of different buildings (usually only 12 of which will be available to buy at any given time), and combined with the fifteen available roles each player has, this game has a ton of replayability. Even though this game has a decidedly lighter feel than its predecessors, people prone to analysis paralysis may struggle over the multitude of options available at any given time.  I was pleasantly surprised. This makes my list of the top 5 2013 games so far.

RATING: 7
I'm growing a little weary of games that ask players to parse a gazillion different choices up front in order to even start the game. Ora et Labora is a prime candidate here. The Glass Road could be described as a hybrid of OeL and Witch's Brew. As such it does have this problem as well to some extent but the buildings are all fairly simple in nature with limited interaction which tones down the issue significantly.

The game's focus is also clearly on trying to predict what actions the other players will choose and participating in those. Again, the implications aren't quite as strong and as pure as in Witch's Brew but what the game sacrifices at one end it gains at the other. Glass Road straddles a comfortable middle ground between those two titles.

RATING: 7
Yet another game from Uwe Rosenberg in which you are supposed to turn resources into buildings which might give you other resources or points; in this case a relatively light game. The unique element in this one is the way you select your actions: you pick five roles to play at the start of a round, and then get to play 3-5 of them during the game, depending on what roles the others pick. There is quite a lot of luck involved, as you early on have to decide where to get your points from, but you cannot know whether the buildings that will come out mesh with your plan. Since the end scores will be quite close anyway, this particular factor has a big influence. It is not a bad game, but totally forgettable. Skip it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The best part of this game is the unique feel of the very euro mechanics. I feel the game is over right before the critical moment of getting everything properly lined up for a good scoring engine. As with a number of euro games, this seems both intentional, and a fault of my play style of trying to set so much up at once. 

The them is very lightly applied to the mechanics of this game, but there are some very interesting mechanics and interaction concepts going on. It is not a game I understood how to play just by explanation. I will need more plays to understand how to score better.

Also, the symbology made my brain weep trying to convert them into mental pictures of how useful each building would be for me.

RATING: 9
I really love this game. The game really improves a lot on the endless piles of resources found in Ora & Labora by using wheels. Your producing brick on one wheel and glass on the other. 

I really like the synergy of how purchasing certain tiles let you manipulate the wheels resources to help you purchase other high value tiles. 

I like all of the Uwe Rosenberg games. You can play it 1 - 4 players and my first game was a solo play. I might have cheated a little but I really had fun just seeing what would happen if I did this or that or this. Wow! That's something else! 

I have read where the "Roofing Company" tile can break the game by setting up some kind of infinite loop to victory, although I've not seen it myself. But I still love this game.

RATING: 9
It has a lot of good ideas that I really like. I like the card idea, I love simultaneous selection in general and the way it's handled in this game is very well done. I love that you are rewarded heavily if you can guess other players' choices and the timing of each card played is important. It adds so much tension and interaction to an otherwise dry euro. The second idea I really like is the resource wheels. They are a clear and helpful, though not quite intuitive, way to track the resources and their conversion, which of course is the main focus of the game. The building system is fine, though most buildings don't seem that interesting at first and clearing your land works well, but it's nothing to write home about. The game is quick and it almost feels like it ends too suddenly, but that's not necessarily a bad thing. Overall, a very solid game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This feels like what I imagine the cave half of Caverna to be, so Caverna must be half Glass Road, half Agricola.  :P  

Glass Road plays quick! Wow.  Some good thinking involved, but so far I've had difficulty squeezing out a victory.  Every game is decided by 0.5 or 1 point.

UPDATE after 3 plays: Okay, I've finished learning the game and identified the few rules missteps we made, but none of them affected how well the game plays.  I dig it.  Nice, fast, thinky... reminds me of Glen More in some ways where buildings and resources chain react.

UPDATE: If you don't buy any production buildings, the game is a very literal affair.  You need clay? You play a card that gets you clay.  I almost traded this game away because of this.  After reviewing the designer's advice in the rulebook to ignore end game score tiles for the first two rounds and only focus on production buildings, we decided to give it another try.  It's a lot more fun being able to convert resources and manipulate your dials on your own in order to get the right combination of resources to afford to build something.  That was a satisfying play.  Satisfying enough that we're interested in playing more, so I've removed it from "For Trade"

UPDATE: after 8 plays I realized we were playing wrong.  Rules for 2p on page 10.  In a 2p game you play more than 3 card rounds.  You play until one player runs out of cards.  Oy.  9th play was so much better.  Feels like the belt was loosened a couple notches. 

UPDATE: 11th play.  Game is great.  Compact, small footprint, but so rich and deep with hard decisions.  We've reached the point where the game is as much about copycatting the opponent's card as it is about figuring out good scoring combos.  Love this game.  My wife picks it all the time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
It looks, feels and plays a lot like Agricola

That said it does have some VERY clever mechanics which Agricola doesn't have such as the way that the rondels manage your resources and you effectively have to turn one of each of the resources in to make glass/brick - that's a neat little idea

Plus the card mechanics are really nice with choosing a hand of 5 and then the way they play out. I like that a lot.

Unfortunately though all the same criticisms I lay at Agricola are still valid. There is too much choice, too many things going on and trying to figure out a strategy in amongst it all is just a bit crazy (unless of course you have an encyclopedic knowledge of the deck and a lot of luck.

I'm upgrading this to a 7. It's definitely Uwe Rosenburgs best game and I should have given it more than the 5.5 I did before. The rondels and the card choices are both very good

RATING: 8
規則:
*起始每個人拿對應15張牌跟對應版圖，內容照對應位置放，建築版塊照背面洗勻，根據人數開對應的主版塊並開對應數量，起始玩家隨機
*資源輪，只要棕色區上方沒東西就會轉，在每次完整行動後轉(一張卡的一個行動就算)
*3~4人流程，每輪從15張選五張，全部一起蓋著出一張，起始玩家開始開，如果此時其它玩家手上有一樣的牌就出出來(蓋著的不算)，所以有該牌的玩家動作(付資源 二選一動作)，如果是唯一一個出該牌的，兩個都可作，此流程進行三輪(跟出的牌上限兩張)
*三張牌的輪次結束，補建築物跟順時鐘換起始玩家，遊戲在四輪後結束，算所有建築分數，最多的獲勝
*三人遊戲時，第四輪有建築物最少得當起始玩家
*隨時行動包括建築物的箭頭轉換跟森林以外的地景移除
*磚邊的建築是基礎建築的升級，會取代掉基礎建築
*如果希望玩久點可以玩五輪
*兩人遊戲 由起始玩家開始輪流出牌，直到有人沒牌出就結束(如果有玩家有三張手牌則可在出一張)，跟出最多也只能出兩張
*solo 玩七輪，每輪選3-4-5-6-3-4-5張牌，選玩牌後洗勻抽一張使用一個能力，直到剩兩張，開起來選一張使用全部能力，上輪選的牌這輪不能選
*拿物品時可選擇少拿，拿食物跟煤礦，該次數量只能選擇反映在一個資源輪，不能拆分
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Yet another recent Euro that leaves you feeling overwhelmed first time you play it. It's based on a clever central mechanism, the 'sweeper arm' that tells you how many resources you have, but the consequences of it moving are hard to forsee. You play cards for action choices, but you may be forced to play them out of sequence if others match your hand. This is a rarity in gaming land, a heavy game with a substantial chaos factor and like the poster child of this genre, Tigris and Euphrates, it won't be everyone's cup of tea.

RATING: 8
A great game with a unique resource mechanic, and while it is prone to some serious AP, gives interesting decisions at every possible point.

The resource generation mechanic is the main attraction here, and is what separates it from the ocean of similar resource management games.  You have two clock faces, each has hands that keep the basic and premium resources separate, and these spin throughout the game to increase or decrease your stocks.  You are always trying to manipulate your supplies in such a way as to keep the clock spinning at a steady rate to ensure constant production of the premium goods which will ultimately score you points.  What I love about this mechanic is that it doesn't necessarily reward somebody who is adept at generating huge amounts of 1 or 2 resources - I have seen a few games before in which you only really need a large stock of money, for example, which can then be traded to skip certain phases of production.  That is not so in this game - you must be producing steady amounts of all the resources to win the game.

The other big part of the game is the action selection round in which you are picking cards one at a time, the other cards in your hand can be played on top of the one selected if somebody else picked any of them for their turn.  This creates a great sub-game in which you are also trying to outguess and second-guess your opponent's moves to maximise the actions you can take on your turn.  This is critical to the game as there are only 4 rounds.

These two great mechanics come together to make a straightforward but very brainburner-y game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
fun, haven't bored of it
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Top 10 all time
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Essentially Agricola without the worker placement, Glass Road manages to be an excellent game anyway. While it will never replace Agricola for hardcore gamers, it does offer a couple of advantages:

1) There are no negative points (from not feeding your people, not having certain resources at game end, not filling in all available spaces), making this one "seem" a lot more forgiving.

2) Everyone starts with the same options (there is no risk of one player having just the right set of cards for a power build), making the game more fair.

The role selection works well as a mechanic, and there is just as much player interaction as Agricola. The game is also beautiful and the resource wheels are great. This one's a real winner!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
this is a nice 1hr medium weight game with a couple of unique(?) mechanics (the production wheels and the way the that the cards are played).

I really like the cards, but the production wheels are a gimmick that adds an unnecessary headache to your plans (the card system only really works at its finest with 4players).

The biggest problem that this game has is the randomness of the building tiles:

1) you can spend the early game accumulating lots of resources in hope that a building will come out that makes great use of them

and/or

2) you can buy vp buildings early and work towards making them work, and hope that any buildings that come out later synergize with what you've already done.

either way, unless you manage to snag a combo early in the game, it's just down to luck as to what you buy works with what you're doing or not.

RATING: 7
2014-03-17	

Initial Rating: 7.0 (March 2014)

I heavily dislike Witch's Brew so when a friend was explaining this to me, I actually thought this was going to be a bad game, however there is not a single Rosenberg game I dislike and this was no exception. I DO like Glass Road a lot (in fact I bought it today).

The game has simple rules, but it also has meaty decisions in a very short time frame (basically which cards to play, the buildings are a lot more obvious). LOVE the resource wheel, I haven’t played Ora et Labora so I’m not sure if the resources are managed in the same way, but I think this is a brilliant design making it very easy to see how many resources you have AND you have a very low upkeep to do in that regard. 

Perhaps Glass Road has more randomness that it is usual in an Rosenberg game, but I need to explore it further to see if this is a negative, neutral or positive thing for me. My guess is that it is a positive thing as the game is so short but it can be negative when big VP buildings appear and you are last in turn.

I dislike that you have to take away a building that allows you to get an infinite loop (this also happened with Caverna), makes me wonder if these games are not thoroughly tested before release. 

Bottom line, it is Witch's Brew done correctly. For now I like it a lot but that can change if I feel the randomness in the buildings is too high. Good euro game. 

Current Rating: 7.0
[hr]
2014-05-06

After more plays, I continue to love Glass Road. A friend asked how could I love this game and hate Witch's Brew. The answer is that in Glass Road you can still play even if another player chose the same role as you, in Witch's Brew you are screwed if another player chose the same role as you and you are first in the turn order. Also the decisions are much more deeper in Glass Road, I don’t think a player can chose roles randomly and do well (I have seen this in Witch's Brew).

The resource wheel is very interesting. It can mislead you easily when you make Glass or Brick as you can easily forget that you spend every other resource you have doing this. I have seen people lose due to this and I really like it, it adds a subtle layer of complexity.

Current Rating: 7.0

This is an enjoyable resource gathering and conversion game. I like the way goods are tracked on the wheels and those also serve to make bricks and glass which are important for buying buildings. It adds some strategy in thinking about what cards the other players may select, but you could get unlucky in your selection. This randomness keeps it from being a great game for my taste, but it goes fast enough that I wouldn't mind an occassional play.

RATING: 8
[gameid=34084] blown up to a full Rosenberg game. Each of the 4 rounds each player chooses 5 out of 15 action cards. Each round three of them get played consecutively with each player again simultaneously choosing one. If one of the hand cards got chosen as action by another player, the action is followed and both players get only one of two possible effects. A very solid and fun mechanism worth more than one boardgame implementation.
It weren't Uwe Rosenberg if he didn't add 6 or 7 different resources, resource conversion and purchase of buildings. The breaking idea is the introduction of the two production wheels for glass and bricks. Sometimes, it's better not to produce the one missing basic resource to get another of the two advanced resources because it will cost another basic resource badly needed.
The random factor is higher than in other big box Rosenberg's and the AP risk in card selection is high as well. Considering the close scores, randomness might be bothering with repeated play but for now I want to play more.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A few things I really like about this one: 
1) It is a faster Euro game that retains much of the Rosenberg feel.  With four players we finished our first game in 1 1/2 hours and this included an AP prone player.
2) There is a lot of variety given the buildings and how the cards play out.
3) Resource management is an interesting twist from other games.
4) Related to the former, this game is unique and adds variety to my collection.  

Things that don't bother me but are worth being aware of:
1) New players can feel directionless and confused by the new game mechanics, especially in how they relate to the goal of the game.
2) It can be harder to find satisfaction in the development of your board compared to games like Agricola, Ora & Labora, and Le Havre.  The beginning board and end board look similar at first glance.

Suggestions: Give it a couple of plays.  The wheel will take a game to figure out and maybe even another to feel comfortable with. New players also need to know that the goal is to get the resources needed to build buildings!  It may seem obvious, but be sure to let everyone know this.  Otherwise the wheel and card selection will feel exceedingly random.

RATING: 6
I’ve decided that I don't like this game.  This is a game that could be great but ends up being just okay and is one that I really don't care if I play it again.  It has great resource wheels and interesting game play from the standpoint of deciding what cards to choose and when to play them.  I like the way the cards work.  Also, it’s a relatively short game which is a plus.  But there are two reasons why I’ve been turned off by this game.  First, although I like the mechanism of playing the cards and taking the actions (either one or two), trying to manage your resource choices ends up involving a good deal of guessing.  The process is stressful (for lack of a better term) and not in an enjoyable or rewarding way.  I usually end up guessing and hoping for the best.  Second, the game design suffers from a trait not uncommon with some other Rosenberg games (Le Havre, Ora et Labora) and that’s the extreme difficulty in being able to read each of the different building tiles from across the table and at different angles.  Although this wouldn’t be much of a problem in a solo or two-player game, with more players it’s quite frustrating.  I usually end up ignoring the public buildings for the most part and just playing the Feudal Lord a lot and managing my own private buildings.  I’ve found that I don’t enjoy playing this game that much (unlike both Le Havre and Ora et Labora) and usually feel glad when it’s over.  I don't need to play this game again.

RATING: N/A
"I'm growing a little weary of games that ask players to parse a gazillion different choices up front in order to even start the game. Ora et Labora is a prime candidate here. The Glass Road could be described as a hybrid of OeL and Witch's Brew. As such it does have this problem as well to some extent but the buildings are all fairly simple in nature with limited interaction which tones down the issue significantly.

The game's focus is also clearly on trying to predict what actions the other players will choose and participating in those. Again, the implications aren't quite as strong and as pure as in Witch's Brew but what the game sacrifices at one end it gains at the other. The Glass Road straddles a comfortable middle ground between those two titles."

RATING: 6
Short game that ends right when you feel you have your engine primed and ready to go.  Be careful collecting resources as the wheel moves and changes quantity of all resources if you leave the lowest spot empty...although that does improve your advanced good.  Feels overwhelming at first due to all the unique buildings.  Card play can be brutal as you must play a card if someone else did and that could occur in a different sequence than you desire as a result.

=====================================================

Kurt R put it so well in his perspective that I'll just quote him:

The game was so constrained a take on the euro as to be lifeless. To play on the "knife fight in a phone booth" analogy, it was a "spork fight in a school cafeteria" with ever present hall monitors to make sure nothing is getting out of hand. Young man, do you have more than 7 wood? Put 3 back! We never have more than 7 of anything here, young man! 

The way the resource rondel works limits you to 3 glass/brick or 7 basic goods, and when you go up in one, you go down in the others. So, I increase my food which then makes all my other stuff go down. And yeah, I get that in theory I'm increasing my glass or bricks but once you max out, you're just lowering your other stuff. You'll see.

I went a "pond strategy" and was able to get a lot of sand and water if I wanted to. Then I got a VP building for 1 VP per 2 water. I had 5 water and could get 5 more, but 7 is the limit, of which only 6 will score for 3 points. And thus ends the Great Pond Strategy. Go to hell.

On the last turn, my decisions were like this: get a VP building to give me 1 VP per 1 wood. Oh nice, I have 5 wood! But I have to give up 2 wood (WTF?) and something else I can't recall but I think maybe a glass which is also a VP. Oh, let me find something else then. I can give up 1 point for 1.5 points or I can take this building that kicks me in the nuts and calls my mom a whore. What to do, what to do... 

So while the card play is mildly interesting, the space for any kind of imaginative play has been bounded so rigidly as to suck the life out of it. It felt joyless. No, seriously, is this all there is? Between this and the re-implementation of Agricola, I seriously wonder if our boy Rosenberg has played his hand. Thanks, Uwe, it was a fun ride.

RATING: 7
I went into this with trepidation: I don't like Agricola, Le Havre, or Ora et Labora. I don't like fiddly-assed microturns that consist of one of 32 steps needed to eventually get a donkey or an oven or whatever. Thankfully Glass Road isn't one of those games. On the contrary, it seems to be aimed exactly at people like me who don't like that stuff. Your actions can't be blocked, although they can be hampered some. There are a variety of ways to get the resources you need, and no resources that do nothing except take up spaces in a chain that eventually ends in something you want. The glass and brick production wheels are pretty clever, especially in the way they're double-edged (you'll want to avoid some resources because otherwise bricks or glass will get made and other resources will be used up.) Not my favorite game in the world, and I won't weep if I never play it again, but nor do I dread it being brought to the table, which is saying a lot when it comes to many eurogames these days.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Interesting game where you have the same set of cards as all other players.  You pick 5 to play and on your turn you reveal a card.  Any other player with same role can take the role too.  If no other player has played that role, you get to take both abilities for that role.  You play three roles, but can match two roles.  

You gather resources to build buildings and score points.  I found it an interesting variant on Ora Et Labora but with the interesting role mechanic.  

I like the game a lot although it can run long with AP prone players.

RATING: 8
muy bonitos los componentes, no es muy extensa la partida, el sistema de diales para obtención de recursos es genial.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another quality design from Uwe Rosenberg. I really love the resource dial mechanic in this, and that's fortunate, since the bulk of the game revolves around figuring out how to get the most out of it.

This features some of the hallmarks of games such as Agricola and Le Havre... agonising decisions, having to rethink carefully laid plans when your options change. What's missing is the sense of building an efficiency engine (efficiency is a factor, and you do build things over the course of the game, but it's not quite the same). This comes through in the way the game ends after an arbitrary 4 rounds (which you can bump up to 5 if you want a longer game). Other Rosenberg games feel "done" when they end, whereas the ending to Glass Road feels more abrupt.

Nevertheless, this is great stuff. Play time is shorter than other Uwe Rosenberg games, and the game is a bit lighter (though still very thinky), which probably makes it a bit more accessible. It seems Uwe Rosenberg has finally figured out how to make a game loaded with resources and choices but without being fiddly and requiring significant set-up/pack-up time, and this is a big plus... I don't have as much spare time now as I did when his earlier games came out, but somehow have still managed to squeeze in quite a few plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
What a nice pick! this game has some brilliant ideas in it, the wheels give a very challenging experience even if the game itself is quite simple to play. Nice combos can be done with the interaction of the various buildings.
I know many people puzzle about this game, since it'd require too much endeavour for what it gives back but I  think they somehow miss the point; this game is not all about building and making your village bigger and bigger, it's about optimizing goods and creating combos and conversion engines. That's the most enjoyable part of the game, imho.

RATING: 8.25
Choosing the cards for "roles" and being able to tag onto other players when they take their actions is interesting. Can be VERY ap prone during the card selection phase. You have to watch what other people are doing to improve your chances of selecting the right cards. If you don't choose well you might find that you could not play all of your cards because the round ends when someone has played all of his or her cards. This means that if you were unable to "tag along" you will be left with unplayed cards in most rounds. That can be frustrating at times for some people because you have to choose cards you might not really want but are certain will be played.

RATING: 8
A marriage of the role selection that I first encountered in Wie Verhext and nifty resource dials. The latter will, at first glance, just represent the automatic exchange of 5 (or 3) basic resources into the advanced resources glass (and bricks), but they turn out to be a bit more tricky in actual use, mostly because the automatic exchange is not something you’re keen on all of the time.

Even with just the basic cards, the two games we played exhibited the variety that will come from different building selections, so replayability should not be a problem here. Analysis Paralysis might become one, but at least our rounds went smoothly. Possibly due to lack of experience. All in all, this was a thoroughly pleasant surprise, especially given how fast it plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Bought at Berlin Con 2016. 
After 8 solo plays: Very quick, puzzly game, about building production chains on your tableau with spatial elements. Feels like a true Rosenberg with some variations.
Interesting resource management with a wheel that automatically transforms base resources into glass or brick, consuming the base resources at the same time. Selecting actions is done via 15 role cards (fisher, builder, etc) of which 5 are choosen each round. Depending whether other players selected the same action you get to do one or two actions provided by that role. This is a nice variation on Ora & Labora by the same author. 
In the solo and two player game action selection is very different but still engaging.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Lighter and faster playing than most recent Rosenberg games. Players have a matching set of action cards and each round choose 5 to play. Only 3 of those cards will be played down in the action rounds but the other 2 may be used if someone else plays them down. The cards each allow you to do two things, you get to do both if you're the only one that plays down that card, otherwise you choose one of the actions on the card. I always enjoy this element of group think. 

Actions are used to purchase buildings. You're trying to build a goods producing engine on your individual playing board that will ultimately generate VPs. After 4 rounds of playing actions the game is over.

Also to mention is an interesting goods wheel that you have to see to understand. If the wheel is empty on the 0 space of the wheel you turn it clockwise and it produces the more advanced goods that are needed to purchase many of the buildings. There's a balance in how you use this wheel to your advantage that I think is the key to game.
