RATING: 7
Half this game feels very Rosenberg-ish: an 80/20 split between Ora et Labora and Agricola. But the other half feels like Witch's Brew or Race for the Galaxy. Correctly predicting what your opponents will select can be worth a nice bonus. Being incorrect can be inefficient or downright punishing, but in the latter case you were probably being too optimistic anyway. A very interesting merge of concepts that results in a clever game. I'm not sure how it stacks up against the designers other games, but it certainly is the shortest and in that context it is certainly worth playing.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This was a nice surprise, as I typically don't like Uwe Rosenburg games. I would probably rank this my 3rd favorite of his behind Patchwork and At the Gates of Loyang. It seems like whenever he isn't doing a farming simulator, I tend to like his modern stuff, for the most part.

Glass Road is pretty simple. It has some San Juan/Race for the Galaxy-like card play along with a little bit of engine building. The main feature is the resource board, which is actually really cool. It reminds me of the gears from Tzolk'in in that it seems like a gimmick, but actually tracks things much easier than if you had to do it manually. The game is a bit on the simple side, though I did enjoy it. It just feels like it's missing...something. I can't quite put my finger on what, but it doesn't quite reach the heights of other great Euros for me.

RATING: 7.5
In this game, players have their own deck of 15 cards, and each round they secretly choose 5 cards to play. Three of the cards will be played; it is possible that your cards will match those selected by the opponent. The cards depict two actions on them and you can do both if no one else chose the card, othewise all players who chose the card get one action. So there is some guess work on what opponents might want to play if you want to get more actions. 

There are 4 rounds, and you try to build VPs. The wheels from Ora Et Labora are used here. Each player has 2 wheels, one with glass, and one with brick. Glass and brick are only earned if all the other resources are advanced by at least one, then the wheel turns, you get less of the resources, but you will have one brick/glass.

It's a quick paced game and I enjoy it. There is a learning curve on all the available buildings. In the game, not all the buildings will come out, so you have to be flexible on your strategy. I'm looking forward to more plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Two crazy mechanisms in this game.  First there are the two goods wheels, brick and glass.  It is quite intuitive because as you gain resources, the 'clocks of the hand' will move whenever you have 1 or more resources of all kinds...when that happens you click the hand over one removing one of every resource, BUT gaining one resource of the Brick or Glass.  Quite intuitive way to show that all these resources are needed to create the Brick, or Glass!

The next mechanism that is quite 'Witch's Brew'-like, is how you select the role cards that will gain you resources and allow you to do things within the game.  Out of 15 cards you select 5 each round, three to play on your turn, and two to play on your opponents turn.  You'd preferably want to know what your opponents plan on doing so that you can play those two cards out of turn, AND play your three cards on your turn without anyone trying to leech, because you will only get a half action when doing so.

Lastly, there is your board where you place your three different types of buildings, Action, One-offs, and end game scorers.  

It all comes together quite good, just wish it was supposed to have ONE MORE ROUND...kind of like most of his games!

2 player: 0:40
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
Another game that had been more than one year in my shelf. This was one of my main interests, but I was waiting for the special moment. Some days ago I saw at BGG that it was most recommended as a 2 players game, so when Joor and me had free 50 minutes this Sunday while everybody was playing something else, we went for it.

[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/play/details/12858598]Played on October 13th, 2014, at Laguna de Duero, with Jooor.[/url]

Rules are easy, but you need some time analyzing the character skills and what the buildings do. then you have to add 2-3 minutes for setup and you're good to go.

Components are very good; there are plenty of them, the cardboard is thick, the wooden tokens have stickers, the weels work perfectly. The box is heavy, and there's no air on it!

It has 2 unique features:
-> Production weels: original, they make the materials accounting much more easy and provide a secondary puzzle for the game. Very nice.
-> Actions are executed by choosing 3 characters out of a selection of 5 for this round. If you're the only one that chose this character, you play 2 actions; if more people chose him, each one only plays one of the actions.

These actions are the meat of the game, and they provide a nice challenge: second-guessing your opponents and deceivethem to get the max out of your turns, but at the same time be able to get everything you need to build the best buildings before them.

Luck is also a factor. Even if the game is a bit thinky, there's no AP, because you all choose your actions at the same time.

The key is that it lasts only half an hour. There are definitly better games out there, but the last 2-3-4 times as much!

I understand why it excels with 2 players: with more, it's more difficult to controkl what everybody is doing, the character coincidences are more luck dependant and it's easier to be stolen of the buildings you want.


[b]First rating:[/b] 7,9

[b]Final result:[/b] 2nd (of 2).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First impression (4 players + 2 players) : I liked the fact rules itself are very clear and easy. On the other hand whole gameplay is very fiddly and chaotic the way it works. There are 15 action cards with each having 2 actions and some of them A or B actions. So total action-pool is rather high. Especially considering you may either do 2 or 1 actions / card depending how other players play ... and part of the game would be to predict how they play to maximize your number of actions you can do. Every player chooses 5 cards but can only play 3 of them but you can leech onto other players cards if you have them in your hand. So you can get benefit of 3-5 cards and depending on how other players play, you can get benefit of 3-8 actions... To make it worse you cannot completely predict WHEN you can make actions as it can sometimes be decided by other players.

So in 4 player game it is rather chaotic, especially the part about when you actually can do actions. In 2 player game the amount of chaos is much less but the outguessing (or more likely just semi-randomly picking something other player is going to play) becomes bigger part of the game.

Gaining resources is rather unique and quite nice mechanism. But when combined to action-chaos it is really hard to plan anything really.

Mixed opinions. All mechanics as-is are cool but amount of (potential) actions and their timings are making it really hard to do any real decisions. It's more like guidelines how this turn is going to play out and I don't like that.

RATING: 10
 [b]Glass Road[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]Still one of my favorite games, and definitely my favorite resource conversion game. Depending on the day either Loyang or Glass Road would be my favorite heavier Uwe game (I'm currently also obsessed with Patchwork which is a much lighter game). This is a game I am always willing to play and it really isn't very difficult to teach. That said it takes a play or two to get comfortable manipulating the resource wheels. I still haven't mastered setting up combos with the different buildings, but it love the amount of variety in the buildings. Especially when you look at all the ways buildings will give you bonus points and then figuring out how to use the different production buildings to maximize your points.The wheel is also an elegant solution to having numerous amounts of resource chits (ala Le Havre) to keep track of. 

I still view glass Road as an under appreciated gem designed by Uwe that plays great with 2 and finishes in under an hour. I can't recommend this game highly enough. My only disappointment is that my wife no longer wants to play, on the flip side my son has actually started requesting plays. [/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (9):[/COLOR]

[i]- Plays great with 2
- Quick playtime: 20 minutes per player is accurate.
- Resource management including how the production wheels work when producing bricks and glass.
- Card play: having to chooses which workers you want to use while also trying to figure out which ones your opponents may want to use (this reminds me a bit of RFtG).
- The buildings that are available from game to game really change your strategy, and make this a game with a lot of replay value.
- Great production quality, love the eater eggs found in the artwork on some of the tiles.

This may not be Uwe Rosenberg's best game design, but it is probably my favorite. A big part of this is the combination of short play time and this is a game my wife has recently been requesting to play. She does not usually like games with lots of moving parts, but after a couple of plays this game has clicked with her. Glass Road is a game that will see consistent play for a long time.

- [thing=167567][/thing][/i]

RATING: N/A
Wonderful game that is very simple to learn and play. The rulebook is very concise. I wasn't quite sure if I was excited about this game or not when I purchased it. I am very glad I did because the game play is very unique and fun. The mechanics are clever and fresh. It reminds me of my first game of Puerto Rico (I am not saying this game is like Puerto Rico at all btw) when I discovered for the first time that everyone had a chance to play on the active players turn. 

Each player has their own deck of 15 cards (exactly the same). Each player then chooses 5 of them to hopefully get to play. When the active player chooses their card to play and someone else has chosen the same card in their hand of 5 cards, they get to play that card too. If the active player is the only one to pick that card, then he gets the benefits of both offers on the card. If someone else chooses the same card then you each choose one of the offers on the card. 

The glass and brick resource wheels are ingenious. They are really an clever aspect and fun part of the game. And the game playing space is kept very neat without piles of resources all over the table. It's all kept neat and tidy on each players own personal resource wheels. 

This game has great replay ability. The piles of tiles that you can build in the game are plentiful and you won't get through them all in one game or even two. You basically build what you can in only 4 rounds, and try to connect your plans through what you are able to buy with resources. Through clever placement of tiles or resource strategies the winner will be the one with the most VP and the VP's won't be huge either.

RATING: 7.75
心得:
主要是玩一人跟兩人
1 2 3~4 人以上規則差異蠻大的
主要是分數都衝不高
機制雖然跟女屋釀有點像 
不過還是些許不同
主要是在第1回合拿個可以轉換建築物
2~3回合規劃拿一個分數建築物並想辦法拿滿分
之後就是選牌上的運氣，能否選到對手想出的牌
規則:
*起始每個人拿對應15張牌跟對應版圖，內容照對應位置放，建築版塊照背面洗勻，根據人數開對應的主版塊並開對應數量，起始玩家隨機
*資源輪，只要棕色區上方沒東西就會轉，在每次完整行動後轉(一張卡的一個行動就算)
*3~4人流程，每輪從15張選五張，全部一起蓋著出一張，起始玩家開始開，如果此時其它玩家手上有一樣的牌就出出來(蓋著的不算)，所以有該牌的玩家動作(付資源 二選一動作)，如果是唯一一個出該牌的，兩個都可作，此流程進行三輪(跟出的牌上限兩張)
*三張牌的輪次結束，補建築物跟順時鐘換起始玩家，遊戲在四輪後結束，算所有建築分數，最多的獲勝
*三人遊戲時，第四輪有建築物最少得當起始玩家
*隨時行動包括建築物的箭頭轉換跟森林以外的地景移除
*磚邊的建築是基礎建築的升級，會取代掉基礎建築
*如果希望玩久點可以玩五輪
*兩人遊戲 由起始玩家開始輪流出牌，直到有人沒牌出就結束(如果有玩家有三張手牌則可在出一張)，跟出最多也只能出兩張
*solo 玩七輪，每輪選3-4-5-6-3-4-5張牌，選玩牌後洗勻抽一張使用一個能力，直到剩兩張，開起來選一張使用全部能力，上輪選的牌這輪不能選
*拿物品時可選擇少拿，拿食物跟煤礦，該次數量只能選擇反映在一個資源輪，不能拆分

RATING: 7.5
Solid design, I like to Simultaneous Action Selection mechanic. Plays quite fast, so good fun / time ratio with little downtime.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Solid design. Standard game in many ways of playing cards to get resources, play cards to build buildings, buildings will help build an engine or score points, most points wins. A bit of focus on engine at the start, but you need to work out quickly how you're going to score your big end-game points as you'll only get 16 or so actions through the game to resource gather and build. So you need to then focus on selecting complementary buildings to boost that scoring mode, and drive single-mindedly towards it. The card/action selection mechanic is interesting ... you can game it a bit by choosing popular choices, and mid-game by choosing both likely choices (to share) and non-likely choices (to get double) that benefit only you. But no doubting there can be a big slice of luck in the order in how they the cards get played out (as well as guessing right in what other people choose). Everything works well, nice components and all. The only thing stopping me rating it higher is the uneven tempo of the game. If a player takes a building you'd focused towards just before your turn, you may need some "minutes" to work out how to readjust your turn and determine whether to change track or not. Because actions are finite, every action wasted (either thru card selection "luck" or this tactical cease-and-replan) can be important, so there can be some paralyzing downtime at times. Interesting to explore, engaging game choices, but not a laugh-a-minute game, one of contemplation and then re-contemplation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
A deceptively simple-looking game, with a ton of depth. Looking at it, it seems like a pretty dry theme (and admittedly I never felt like an 18th century Bavarian glass worker while I was playing), but it's really a fantastic game. 

What seems like simple resource management becomes quite a bit more complex with the rondel mechanic that automatically creates glass and bricks the moment you have enough resources. Sometimes you have plans for that charcoal, but then you got some sand, and now you have more glass and less of everything else!

The building tiles add even more depth to the game. You won't use more than 5-6 each game, and with 92 of them included in the box, you'll never run out of combinations!

RATING: 7
Glass Road is a surprisingly fast resource management game that features simultaneous action selection.  Players choose which actions they're going to use on their turn, and then reveal those actions in three rounds of play--but if someone else has the same action, both players get a lesser version of that action immediately.  Planning is difficult in this game, because essentially you can't be sure in which order your actions will happen.  This, and the fact that buildings can be unintentionally sniped by other players can make this a little bit of a frustrating experience sometimes.  I still found the game enjoyable, but you really have to be into contingency planning.

(4 plays)

RATING: 7
Glass Road is a really nice change-of-pace from the recent Rosenberg wheelhouse (which I tend to like). Each player has the same deck of role cards and selects which he or she will play during one of the four rounds of the game. That selection, along with the timing of playing them, determines whether the player does one or both of the actions on the role card. This all leads to a nice interactive bit of the game, where a player is trying to get inside the head of his opponents. The rest of the game is a pretty standard resource-converting-to-resource-converting to points affair. That said, the whole package is nicely put together, plays relatively quickly, and made for a really good euro experience. I will be very happy to play this one some more.

RATING: 5
Resource conversion puzzle, the main aim being to trade in to draft buildings to place on your tableau. Buildings either provide conversion benefits, one off benefits or for the required vps (fixed or variable) to score after 4 rounds of play.

One problem to address is that your tableau board is already occupied with forest, vegetation, ponds and sand pits, which provide resources if discarded or kept.

The mechanism that allows you to gain, discard and build is similar to that in Witches Brew in that you select 5 from your hand of cards (same as everyone else). One of the selected cards is place face down, then in turn a player declares the face down card. If you hold the matching one in your hand you get to play it. There are two forms of kicker here: if you are the only one to select that 'role', trade in the resource if necessary and you get to do both listed actions on the role, otherwise you only get choose one and the other player(s) choose one as a bonus. This was one of the few aspects of game I liked as it can elicit your greed, causing some emotional tension and drama in the denial. Also the reveal is the fun part with the coincidence of selections.

The other aspect is deducing which of the 5 cards other players will pick. Your source of information is not only the busy tableau or building draft board, but mainly the players individual resource inventory board, which is the novel part of the game. Resources gained and paid are marked with discs moving forward and back around a wheel, but the novelty lies in the fixed clock hands which move according to gaps left by the leading or last resource(s). This movement generates or uses up a very valuable resource: glass on one wheel and brick on the other (hence Glass Road). 

Strangely all the mental work involved, scores are unit increments so are close (which I like), but again the scores are fractured, variable and scattered so no tension there unless you wish to do the maths.

Playing with 4 makes for a resource conversion, deduction headache, and the overall feel is it seems too chaotic and difficult to plan, and it doesn't help with the wheels being a challenge as you discover that gaining a resource going last forces the clocks hands to "manufacture" glass/brick and reducing your moved resource to zero. Curse that efficiency!!! Maybe interested in playing with 2 (BGG recommendation) as the theme is there almost, but I suspect it isn't my taste, with Gates of Loyang being a strong contender (being more thematic) for a Rosenberg farming design I'm happy to play.

RATING: 10
This is a very quick playing, very minimalistic resource management game. Each player gets a wheel with resource tokens to indicate how much of each thing you have, a set of cards identical to other players, and a player board with some spots for buildings and more covered spots you can clear off. Each player then selects one action from their hand, and you get a bonus for playing a card in a round that nobody else has left in their hand, and if they do have the card, they get to get half the effect. It creates a really interesting push your luck element, but it can be difficult to keep track of without several plays under your belt.

The game is also extremely short. In my group we have some AP prone players, and it takes an average of an hour and a half including rules explanation. I felt overwhelmed by this game on our first few plays, but now I'm getting the hang of it and enjoying it more and more.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::star: Raw enjoyment

RATING: N/A
Glass Road is very clearly a Rosenberg engine, only with none of the fussy bookkeeping overhead. Prior to this, handling chits and cubes and making change were all necessary parts of the overhead for a Rosenberg game. Ora et Labora kind of hinted at something tidier, but unfortunately the cool resource wheel in OeL was still attached to a plodding multihour competition in strategic component sorting. 

Glass Road still sees you dragging wood and peat through a flowchart searching for clay and food, but you're not manually handling anything. This cuts just a [i]huge[/i] amount of non-value-add stuff from the game. And the forced production is really, really good. Not only does it prevent resource hoarding, but it also helps the game actually end in a reasonable amount of time. 

Even at 2p, the cardplay matters, though I'm in a strange place with it: it'd be more interesting with three players, but then you'd have someone else to extend the game. I don't know that I'd be cool with this if it took much longer. But unlike a lot of Euros where you want to Do What Your Opponents Are Not Doing, in Glass Road you want to get a little close to their strategy but not too close. That's a knife edge. I'm totally fine with those.

A game that I am totally considering buying.

RATING: 7
I love the piggybacking in this game. Per period you pick five cards from 15 and per round each player chooses a card. First player announces his first role card and if you have that card in your hand, you can do an action too.

These two are nice mechanics and the game flows fast. The down time is in studying the buildings that can be built. I think there are way too many.

RATING: 7
I've come to the conclusion that Uwe's games tend to be too fiddly for me.  I like medium-heavy Euros and appreciate his games for their balance and strategic elements, but I'm getting tired of moving 243252 pieces around the table.

This game, specifically, has a similar feel to some of his others (Caverna, Fields of Arle, Ora et Labora, etc).  If you enjoy those, you'll enjoy this.  If you don't enjoy those, you probably won't enjoy this.

I'm perfectly fine playing this game again and again...but I can think of dozens of other games I'd rather setup and play (mostly because the setup of Uwe's games just takes too long).

RATING: 8.58
A super tight game that is really simply with respect to rules:  choose 5 helpers and go back and forth and chose one to play for three rounds.  If you play some one that someone else chose and they are in their hand they get to play it.  Do this four times and see who wins.  This game seems to need a lot of plays to get comfortable on what to do.  It just seems so wide open.  The resource manager wheel are ingenious.  They take down a ton of resource book work but also bring a dilemma  when with their automatic production.   Just a ton to digest with this one and has a really unique feel to it.  Tons of potential for this games rating to rise. 

Update:I bumped this one up a little more because it surprisingly plays as a satisfying experience as a solo game.  Not to heavy and plays in an hour or less.  Pretty quick to set up.  It has interesting decisions while still in the spirit of the game.   Obviously no interaction but there is more tactics brought in as cards come up a bit random so you may have to adjust.  Really liked this.

RATING: 7
I've played this about four or five times since receiving it for Christmas in 2013, including games with 2, 3 and 4 players. At first I was a bit disappointed, but now I think I like the game. It is extremely quick, much quicker than I expected - without rules explanation, I really can't see this taking much more than half an hour. That is no bad thing, of course - but it has more components than many 2-3 hour brain-burners, so setup takes about as long as the game itself! Add in rules explanation (which takes a fair while, even though the basics of the game are simple - but you have to explain how the production wheels work, how the card rounds go, what actions you can do at any time, what the different type of buildings are, describe what the different cards do...), and the game itself seems to take up only a fraction of the time this game is on the table. It is also very hard to plan for anything very much, at least early in the game, and while there is certainly an element of trying to anticipate which cards your opponents will choose, most of the time you're thinking more about how to make sure you can build the buildings you need at the right time, while also trying to maximise your score from the ones you've already built (or plan to build later). Definitely more a tactical game than a strategic one.

I thought I would really love this game, and I definitely like it. But no-one else I've played the game with has seemed that taken with it, and it falls awkwardly between the two stools of light filler and challenging strategy game. For that reason, as well as the inevitable arrival of new games to claim my attention, this is now very rarely a game I would actively suggest to play. But I do still enjoy it on the very occasional times when it does come out.

RATING: 5
A quick game of selecting a hand of roles that do things, then playing them out, and getting the full effect if you were only one that picked the role, and only half if someone else picked the same role (though you select more roles than you get to play, so you want to pick some to collide with other players to get free actions).  It offers a paralyzing amount of simultaneous choices—pick 5 of 15 roles, then pick which you want to play, keeping in mind which buildings you want to buy and resources you want to generate which are converted to other resources via a fiddly wheel mechanic.  Your ability to predict what will actually happen is limited though, given the complexities of interacting with other players's choices.  It felt like a lot of work for fairly little reward.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Another value packed game by Uwe Rosenberg - 16 punch boards (and some assembly work), pack this box again to the brim. There's just no room for an insert, so obviously the publisher is saving a ton of money there.

It plays very nicely. The Ora et Labora resource wheel is there in duplicate with wheels representing your glass and brickworks. Your mind will bend as you try to boost your all important glass and bricks without screwing up the rest of your stock of raw resources. Lovely.

However the hook for this game is the card playing mechanic, which is simple and yet intriguing. Pick five out of 15 cards a turn and if you can play a card nobody else has in their hand then you'll get to utilise both actions on it. If someone else has the card then you both get to utilise one of the actions on the card. So optimally over the three direct card plays per turn you'll be looking to play three unique cards and pick two others you're sure your opponents will be playing. Simple mechanic, but hard to do. It's not at all blind bidding because there's some real deduction and second guessing going on.

The theme is an interesting one. A huge bonus is that the game plays in about an hour once you know what you're doing and it gets a little ratings boost for this fact alone. Turns are fast and engaging, with some agonising decisions throughout the game, but especially as the game draws to a conclusion. That said, the downtime is minimal, although I guess this could depend upon your group. Points are simple to calculate. We have a winner!

The only possible downsides I can see is that some of the building combinations are hugely powerful, but it should be nothing that aware opponents can't attempt to level out if they are on top of their games. The rulebook is also a tad verbose and makes searching for things slow on a first play as points dealing with one particular aspect are spread over multiple non consecutive pages. You won't need to refer to it after the first play though.

-----------
iOS version: Just about one of the worst apps I've come across. Never been able to finish a game - the game has frozen half a dozen times - frozen as in frozen. I've waited a few minutes for the AI to think, or for something in the background to do something, but after five minutes I declare it to be "frozen". A pity.

RATING: 7
The inherent chaos of simultaneous role selection typically relegates the mechanism to dime-a-dozen filler games, but Glass Road breaks this mold by incorporating it into a more substantial game. Much like in [thing=34084][/thing], players here [i]want[/i] some degree of clashing with their opponents, in addition to a few unique picks. There are also other design nuances to consider as well, such as dealing with cards which are flushed out prematurely from your hand.
 
Being a Rosenberg game, there is also an inevitable element of resource management and conversion. Manipulating the resource wheel is initially a little brain-bending, but actually works very well. All this is brought full circle by the conversion of resources into points, represented here by buildings. The overall design smartly ensures that getting more actions through role selection isn't a clear advantage; the right buildings must be purchased or any extra actions are otherwise wasted.

Glass Road is fairly light, decent Euro fare. The card selection mechanism outshines the resource management bit, but both parts really complement each other well. It would be interesting to see a similar mechanism implemented in a lighter filler though. The rules overhead for the game may be on the high side for a game which can be done in about an hour.

RATING: 7
Picked this up because I'm a sucker for simultaneous action selection. Plays as fast as everyone says it does, with a very high density of decisions. Michelle seems to spend a lot of time deciding which buildings to make (even when it's HER builder!), which is a little annoying. It does feel a little short with only 4 building periods. 

I've never played Witch's Brew (since it doesn't play with 2 at all), so the action selection was pretty novel to me. It is pretty neat and brainburn inducing, although the incentives are a little different in 2-player than multiplayer. For 3- and 4-player games, you pretty much always want 3 unique and 2 matching. In 2-player, the second player wants the same, while the first player actually does better with just one matching.  

There is a surprising amount of interaction for a non-confrontational game, and I like that it forces you to keep a close eye on what your opponent is doing. 

The wheel isn't so much a mechanic as it is just a bookkeeping tool (of course, the glass and brick resources had to be designed with the wheel in mind, or none of it would work). It serves its purpose well and helps to keep the game moving.

玻璃之路
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
The production wheel is an awesome idea. Other than that, it's an Uwe Rosenberg with another variation of the by now well-known theme -- producing goods to convert to other goods to produce more goods to build buildings to produce more goods. Thanks to the production wheel a lot less on the fiddly side than usually, but also more centered about exactly two goods (glass and brick) as opposed to the more open approach of earlier games. I'm not too fond of the forced production of glass and brick either, which in my opinion just needlessly complicates the micro-optimization, but that can be easily remedied with a house rule.

The big difference though is the action selection mechanism with the (not always desired) following of other players. I'm badly undecided what to think of that one -- on the one hand, it makes the game much more interactive than the usual simple competition for common resources (buildings, ...). On the other, it can completely mess with your plans in a way that dangerously leans towards the too unpredictable side.

RATING: 6.5
1 play, 3er

The latest from Uwe Rosenberg. This one, though, plays much more quickly (maybe 60 min once you know the game?) while still giving you much to think about. The production wheel, originally seen in Ora et Labora, has been cleverly tweaked to manage resource collection and goods production: once you have enough of all the required resources, the are automatically consumed (1 each) to produce the advanced good (brick or glass). Like in Le Havre, the bazillion cards of Agricola have been cut way back, to roughly 30. They are now building tiles, only a random subset of which will be available each game. And the terrain stuff in Ora is also seen here, although simplified: several tiles on your player board, ability to acquire more.

Something brand new: a personal deck of action cards from which players select 5 each round (and play at least 3). 

Cool game, but:
- like Loyang, it's a low-VP game (around 20) so every half VP is crucial. 
- choices at the beginning seem overwhelming, with little guidance as to which cards to choose
- the production wheels feel vaguely like the Trajan circle: difficult to figure out how to manage it at first!
- it seems as if, to do well, you really have to figure out what actions other people are choosing (so you can piggy back onto them, getting up to 5 actions instead of your base 3)

I do, however, LOVE the production wheel -- so clever! A very nice refinement of the system!

I would like to try it again, but I'm not necessarily crazy about it right now. Maybe it's just a first-game thing, but it seemed too random as to whether or not you got bonus actions from other people's card choices.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
1-4, 75m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Some games really are simply made by their mechanics.  This is one of those games.  Sure, the goal of the game is really to build structures that award victory points but the gameplay that is in service to this goal is what really shines for me.

There are two very original mechanics in this game that offer a breadth of interesting decisions and puzzles to tackle.  The first is the resource wheels each player has that manages the number of each resource they collect.  That phrase in and of itself doesn't make for anything original but each wheel is composed of two parts, one representing a refined good (glass or brick) and the other half representing all the goods that go into creating that refined good.  As soon as you have 1 or more of all of the unrefined goods (and as long as you haven't reached your maximum 3 of the refined good) the wheel moves on its own to create glass or brick and lowers the number of the other resources until either one hits zero or the refined good reaches its maximum.  On first inspection this may not seem like a problem, we want those refined goods, don't we?  Of course, but we also need many of the unrefined goods to build buildings (don't forget the goal of the game) and also fuel the abilities of those buildings.  The turn of the resource wheels cannot be stopped so often you can even find yourself getting some number of a good you need only to find it immediately converted into glass or brick and dropped back down to zero if you're not careful.

This is only one half of what makes this a very original game.  The other half is the card play that makes up most of the actual activity in this game.  Each player has a collection of 15 cards that allow them to collect resources, build buildings, and manage their player boards.  In each of the 4 rounds of the game players will select 5 of these 15 cards then across 3 turns play 3 of the 5 selected cards.  The interesting piece is that each card has two abilities on it.  If, when you play your card, no other player has that same card in their hand (either because they also played it that turn or they didn't select it as one of their 5) you can use both abilities.  But if another player does have that card in their hand then they get to play it "for free" and both players only get to use one of their abilities.  This means most rounds you are trying to choose 3 cards your opponents aren't going to so you get the most efficient use out of them while also selecting two cards your opponents will to hinder them and also get a couple free effects in the deal.  But there's even more to it than that.  With enough experience you can identify even more clever ways to interact with your opponent such as playing a builder role early in a round to force your opponent to remove it from their hand before they get a chance to collect the resources they need.

Like I said, technically the goal is to get victory points through the building of structures but I get so wrapped up in these very clever and puzzley game mechanics that it's even easy to lose sight of that goal and just have fun with how the game is played.  The one negative criticism I will levy on the game is that it feels way too short and most of the buildings in the game max out at too low of a point total to really make any gains.  This results in a very truncated game feel where you didn't get to do enough and final scores all within a couple points of each other.  This is fixed easily enough though by adding a few more rounds to the game, I think.  Double the number of players seems perfect actually for 3-4 players (6 or 8 rounds instead of the suggested 4) because that evens out the number of times each player will have to play in each position.  8 rounds might feel a bit long though, I would have to test it out myself first.  But at 4 (and with 3 players) the game finished in under an hour and felt way too short.

Other than that one small gripe though (which just meant I didn't get ENOUGH) this is a fantastically clever game.  Another home run by one of my favorite designers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
If you had told me that I'd play 2 new-to-me Ooooo-va Rosenberg games within a week (this and "Wurfel Bohnanza") and enjoy both of them, I'd wonder if you were smoking your Agricola harvest.  The whole "collect resources to buy stuff that either allows you to collect resources more effeciently or gives you victory points" is standard Euro-game fare (though very smoothly done in this particular game), but of course the aspect that turns everything on its head is those resource dials!  Rarely do even experienced gamers find such truly unique mechanisms in games!  Those things make managing resources a real mind-bender.  Everything else in the game is pretty straight-forward and plays out quickly (which I appreciate), though exactly how to tie everything together provides quite a challenge.  I also enjoy how the role selection goes down, and how identical character selection causes reduced effects (just see if I don't put a brick through your glass!).  The physical components are all quality products with pleasant artwork, so no complaints there.  This one gets a "thumbs up" from someone who doesn't often enjoy them Euro-type games.  Game design: 4.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 3/5  Total: 7.5/10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Glass Road reminds me a lot of another recent release, Francis Drake, in that it marries a common kind of efficiency game (there worker placement, here the typical Rosenberg convert-things-into-other-things-with-one-million-unique-buildings) with a strong element of simultaneous second-guessing. In essence, action cards have two abilities, but if any other players have chosen the same card then each can use one of the abilities only. Much of the mental effort goes towards attempting to use both actions on your own cards and while also leeching from other players.

The convert-things-into-other-etc part of the game is a shallower retread of Ora et Labora, with similar resource wheels (though now per-player) and minor spatial considerations. While long conversion chains are one of the things that make other Rosenberg games interesting -- successfully building an engine to produce steel in Le Havre, for example, demands long-term planning -- Glass Road has only two level two resources, glass and bricks. In addition, buildings are drawn face down and players have no way of knowing whether one will even be available. The result is a far lighter and more tactical game.

Ultimately I feel precisely as I do with Francis Drake: the most interesting part is the second-guessing involved in action selection, but I'm fairly sure I would rather play a smaller game -- like R or Donburiko -- that dispenses with the tedious victory point conversion and packs the same tension into 1% of the time.

Ratings history: 6 -> 5.5

RATING: 8.5
Oh! My! God! This game is so tight. 4 rounds with 3 actions each (sometimes a bit more). And half the time you will only perform half the action. There is SO little time to get stuff done. The only way to play this is to choose a path on turn one and stick to it, or you will get no where. At least, that is what it felt like in this first game.
This game again has the resource dials we've seen in a couple of other Rosenberg games, but they work differently in this game, with production of glass and brick automatically happening when you produce certain other resources. A fun puzzle in and of itself.
Gameplay is quite fast, rules are quite simple, but game play seems to offer meaningful decisions. And with the huge stacks of buildings, replay value should be very high.
I was pleasantly surprised by this one, even though my score of 12.5 was quite miserable.

RATING: 7.6
interesting game, a lighter Uwe Rosenberg game
interesting mechanics (resource wheels, choosing+matching of role cards, landscape crafting with tiles, available buildings give strategies)

The resource wheels are a bit odd. If you think you have all resources, production consumes resources until maxed out or one resource is back to 0. Makes it rather hard to plan resources.

I like the mechanic with the role cards, choosing 5 from 15 in order to play 3 that no other player has chosen and 2 to try to match what another player plays. That allows for some light mind games. Only problem is that it sometimes takes too much time to make the decisions because it is hard to get an overview over the 15 cards plus offered buildings and resources.

The landscape crafting is also nice, but I think it's not fully balanced.

I like that different strategies open up depending on the available buildings. You basically need to choose a strategy that is (1) available, (2) do-able with the given actions and resources, (3) that none of the other players does and (4) that doesn't conflict with what else you do/did. At the very least it's a tough planning exercise. ;)

Only 4 rounds makes it tough to get something build up. Resources are usually very tight.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Possibly my favorite Rosenberg action-selection game in terms of mechanics (I enjoy Agricola's theme more). The game is tightly focused and quicker playing than other similar offerings. As in a game like Le Havre, Glass Road revolves around selecting actions and maximizing points via the purchase of buildings. And like many of Rosenburg's games, having a solid understanding of all the buildings ahead of time is the only way to approach the game skillfully. This is a negative for me because I am asked to play and learn these games on the fly -- and I do not have a strong desire to study the game when not playing. That said, there is much to like in Glass Road, especially if you are a fan of designers like Rosenburg or Feld.

Of note is the action selection mechanic, which forces you to anticipate what others might play. This helps ameliorate the feeling of multi-player solitaire. Also interesting are the resource dials, which are unintuitive at first, but then reveal themselves as a brilliant way to emphasize and streamline the collection of the two most important resources: glass and brick.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Really good! When this first came out I thought it looked like another generic euro game and a lot of reviewers seemed to think that as well. The reality is that they were right - it's a pretty basic euro - but it has a few strong selling points.
You're gathering resources and turning them into victory points just like in any other euro. But the card play mechanic and the resource gathering, via a dual resource wheel, both feel like fresh takes on the genre.
You each select five cards and you'll get to play at least three of your cards every round. If you play a card that somebody else has in their hand, they get to play that card out of turn, meaning they basically get free extra actions and it's possible to play all five cards each round if you're lucky. You're playing cards in order to gain resources that allow you to build buildings, buildings which can dictate how many points you'll score at the end of the game. When you gather resources you have to plan carefully because some resources will produce automatically and consume other resources in the process thanks to the production wheels.
The simplicity is also a big plus. The cards and buildings are all straightforward and easy to read with minimal iconography. After an initial learning game, the rulebook has only been needed for the occasional reference.
The game also doesn't overstay its welcome. A game tends to last around an hour even with three or four players.

Overall this game was really a pleasant surprise.

RATING: 8
Got this one for my birthday. Excited to get this to the table.

::UPDATE:: 4/21/2015

So, this game has grown on me. What I like about it is how much brain burning it packs into such a short time frame. What I don't like about it is how much brain burning it packs into such a short time frame. Basically, if I'm in the mood for it, I'll be craving it. But if I'm not, I want to stay far away from it. 

Why is this? I think--after analyzing it plenty in my mind--it's because I feel like there's very little wiggle room. I recognize that the combos that you can create from the variety of tiles in the game create a game space for endless possibilities, but all those possibilities seem to feel very much the same to me, and they all feel restricted(?). I don't know the word for it, but it's there. It's super tight, and not calculating right, not planning well with your action cards, and all that can feel really devastating when you don't do it well. On the other hand, it's very satisfying when you play it right/well.

I'll be keeping it, because there are times when it's all I want to play. And the fact that my niece and I can whip out a game from opening the box to closing the box in less than 40 minutes is amazing. 

I'm giving it an 8 for now. With further plays, it has the potential to climb.

RATING: 5.5
First Impression:

A med.euro with a deduction mechanic!

This Frankensteins marriage works. Warning, this is not just another euro where guessing what your opponent wants to do is helpful because you can prepare for it or hinder it. In this game you get some real advantages if you guess correct and confuse your opponent from guessing. Actually that's the game, the rest is there for flavor, variety and different strategies - but nothing gives you the edge as outguessing your opponents actions. The game works, but I can see several players being frustrated by this mechanic.

I liked it, but I'd like to play more to comment more on the available strategies lying below the deduction/bluffing layer. This game is definately unique however.

RATING: 7
Glass Road is a bit of an awkward duck for me.  It feels like two different games.  The first half of the game is a card game where you're trying to predict what cards other people will choose.  The goal being that you pick 3 cards that no one else will and picking 2 that other's will.  This phase of the game is a little bit random and luck-based.

The second half of the game is a resource gathering and building game, more typical of what you'd expect of Uwe Rosenberg.  Some buildings give you on-going abilities, some of them have an immediate effect and some give you points at the end of the game.

My main problem with this game is that this game feels like it wants to be two different things.  On one hand it wants to be a solid resource gathering game where you build stuff... and on the other it wants to be a social deduction card game.  If it's going to be the former, then guessing which cards will be played is a little too random a way to determine if you're going to gather resources or have the chance to build.  And if it wants to be the latter, then you really don't need a complex building game tacked on.  Additionally, it's a little random if the right buildings come out for you or not.

I enjoy the game, but I'm not sure about whether it's a keeper for me or not.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Well, I went to play this game after a promise of its teary owner that he could explain it in 5 minutes and that we would play in 40. Soon, the other player and I learned that the owner used trickery - he did explained in under 5 minutes, but he defended himself saying the time didn't included the 15 cards nor the buildings. We accepted this mainly because the game was all set and ready to go. But, be warned to not fall in the same trick! :)

So, the basics of [thing=143693][/thing] are: everyone has his own player board, filled with forests, glades, sand pits and ponds. Also, every player has a hand of 15 cards - they are all unique among themselves, but every player has the same ones. Of these, each round, you will choose five. After this, every player picks one of these 5 cards and place it face down in the table. The first player of the round reveals his card, and, if any other player has the same card [u]in hand[/u] it must be revealed. If no one has the card that was revealed in hand, the player uses both the actions of the card (every card has two, except one). If even one player has the card revealed in hand, the players that revealed the card and everyone else that had the same card in hand, can use one of the actions in the card (every player decides which, they don't have to be the same one). After everyone has had 3 turns (not countings the turns in which you used a card in your hands to "follow" other players - but there is a limit of 2 cards you can "follow", since these, together with the 3 yourself will use, will count for all the 5 cards you had chosen), the round is over. For the next rounds you can always choose the same cards you did before (you don't have to cycle the cards in any way - you are always free to choose the ones you want). The cards do actions that involve acquiring resources and build things (even new glades, sand pits and ponds). Buildings demand some resources to be built (of course) and there are three types of them: the ones with one-time, immediate effects; the processing buildings (turning something into something else); and the ones that give points in the end of the game (for a great deal of different things).

You gather resources, mainly to be able to do two things: turning them into bricks or glass - each player has another board, with two wheels, one to make bricks, other to make glass. When the wheel spins (because you had advanced all the basic resources types), it signifies that you had turned the basic resouces (like sand, water, clay, and others) into bricks or glass - is a learning experience in how difficult is to make these damn things! And, of course, the best buildings tend to require bricks and glass to be made, and as you can sense these resources are difficulty to have, since, for the wheel to turn, you have to have a clean spot in the wheel, meaning you have 1 or more of all the basic resources in that wheel (and, this means that you lose 1 or more of every basic resource).

So, the game goes like this - with you trying to pick cards that no one else will (because you will do both actions on it), and also trying to see which they will choose, for you be able to get one or two free actions. There are some timming balances to be made: the order in which you choose to activate the cards matters a lot. The buildings can help you do many things, and usually will provide you a north to follow through the game, so it is usually a good move to build or use the Landord (I guess it is his name) to reserve three buildings. Because almost (if not, in fact) everything in the game can be worth points, you just have to have the correct building to make this happen for one or other thing. So, at first, you have to use the buildings open to be built to trace some sort of strategy, or you won't know if you build more glades, or more pits, or ponds, or get a lot of clay, or charcoal, or glass, or, or, or, or...

The game proceeds for 4 rounds, and, in the end, the person with the most points wins.

[thing=143693][/thing] seemed, in a first play, as a fine game, reasonably fast (but not the 40 minutes promised, it is, indeed, more what says in the box: 20 minutes per player), and all the way through I felt like I was being sucked by quicksand from one of the pits. You have so few actions (it can be up to 32 actions, but can also be as low as 12) that I don't even had the feeling of actually accomplishing something. My board was really similar to what it was in the start - minus two forest areas, and plus a few glades and two buildings. Way less change than the one I saw in [thing=70149][/thing], which is [thing=143693][/thing] older brother, and in that you will definitely have a bunch of buildings and a completely different board as the one you had at the start. [thing=143693][/thing] seems brutally fast and tight!

The rules are fairly easy (only the use of the wheel required some examples to understand, but once it clicked, no further explaining was necessary), and all the 15 cards are simple to grasp what they do. The buildings demand more attention, since there are a lot of them (+90), and constantly new ones appear throughout the game. [thing=143693][/thing] also has a variant, in which the game last for 5 rounds instead of 4. This can be a good thing, in order for some strategies that require more time to take place - it sures changes the tempo of the whole game (and, of course, makes it longer). My view, as up to now, it that the overall experience will be better by playing 5 rounds.

So, to sum it up, [thing=143693][/thing] is a good game, but, pondering about my play, I put it behind [thing=70149][/thing], which is a more complex and long game, sure, but also better. ([thing=143693][/thing] also stays behind [thing=31260][/thing], but [thing=70149][/thing] is way closer in feel and some of the actions.) Of course, the main advantage of [thing=143693][/thing] is its greater acessibility, because of the shorter playing time and easy to learn rules.

RATING: 10
Currently my favourite game to play in any situation. 

It's simple enough to teach people who otherwise only know about gateway games, and short enough for them to not be intimidated, but it's a beautiful strategic machine that will engage heavy euro gamers entirely. The most common remark I get from the latter group is "Wow, that was satisfying, but I didn't have to spend all night on it, and it felt really good to not have to feed everyone." Needless to say, in that last bit they were mainly comparing it to Agricola, also by Uwe. I think most people gloss over most of the games he's made, only going for the most popular ones. This gem is one reason you should dig deeper.

I will always make time for a game of Glass Road. I only have to make an hour, after all! Once the app's online multiplayer is working correctly, maybe ask to challenge me :)

10/10

RATING: N/A
Bavarian glass makers. Player boards represent the clay works area.  A central board determines random tiles that provide production or scoring benefits.  Based on the starting upgrade tiles we select a sub set of specialist cards for each of four years.  The production wheels are a very clever tracking system for managing your brick and glass that FORCES conversion.  The evolution of gaming.  This game shows how rondels will replace the forest of wood cubes in a box.  The free action mechanic by anticipating the other players' card play is brilliant.  Intuitive game play that meshes with the theme.  This is a fast game but we still feel satisfied at the end.  Without a huge time commitment it is much easier to demand a re-match! The trick at the start is to figure out the best combination of tiles to invest.

RATING: 9.2
Keeping a typical Uwe Rosenberg-theme but not using worker placement but hand management as it's core mechanism. The card mechanism and the hand management really make this game shine. The cards all have two functions but, depending on which cards the other players use you might only get to use one of those. So you often are not fully able to do what you had in mind.
At the end of a game you always feel like you just needed one more round to get things going, so this really keeps you wanting to play this game again.

RATING: 7.9
A new game from Uwe Rosenberg. A lot of people will stand up and cheer when they hear something like this. Not us. Why? Well, because we kind of have a hate-love relationship with this designer. He designed some great games, but other games he designed we don’t like. That’s why we always want to try a new game by this designer before we buy it. When we were invited to play Glass Road, we were not that enthusiastic. The game looks a lot like Agricola, and it’s no secret that we don’t like that game. But with Le Havre in the back of our minds, we decided to give it a go. The game was explained to us, and soon we were playing the first of four rounds. As most of the time, it will take a little while before you get into the mechanics of the game. Halfway the game, all of the players were thinking the same thing. It’s one of those Uwe’s again. Not a fun game. Not bad, but certainly not a good game.

But then something happened. We really got into the game and it’s nice mechanics. The game began to shine. After finishing the game, I wanted to play it again. This is not a heavy brainburner like Le Havre or Agricola, but it’s also not a family game. One minor thing about this game is that when you’re playing a four-player game with slow players you will suffer from a lot of downtime. In other words, this game needs the right group to play it. Best number of players for this game might be just 2 players.

RATING: 7
I would've liked to play this more but my wife hated it so that was never going to happen. The resource wheels are an interesting, but also somewhat frustrating, puzzle to figure out. The small iconography was also a bit of a pain to read. Overall, the mix of card play and resource management, combined with a quick but still thinky game experience, was appealing and probably meant that I would've kept this around, at least for a little while. But on the other hand, I'm not sad to see it go either.

RATING: 9
Takes the euro model in which one purchases buildings that give actions/windfalls/endgame points, then punches it up with an excellent simultaneous action selection mechanic. One of my top ten games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Brilliant.  The auto-manufacture of goods hearkens back to Roads & Boats - an absolute favorite - but the ingenious way in which the production wheels handle it makes even the ingenious wheel in Ora et Labora look clumsy.

Also, we finally have blind simultaneous action selection that doesn't completely suck.  In fact, it really, really works well.

I can't wait to play this more and more and experience the overload of buildings.

RATING: 8.5
Que no sea de los grandes no tiene nada que ver con la calidad de la diversión que ofrece. No es un plato fuerte dirigido a saciar, colmar la glotonería propia para luego hacernos reposar y degustar una lenta digestión. Es más bien como una taza de té, mesurada, cuyo sabor es sorprendentemente intenso pero cuyo placer termina en cuatro pequeños sorbos, cuatro turnos llenos de decisiones, de tramar tu estrategia y engañar al contrario. 

La taza siempre termina demasiado pronto como para hacer todo lo que uno desearía, pero afortunadamente su corto placer invita a servirse de otra partida y a seguir disfrutando de este título que bien tiene cabida entre sus hermanos mayores, no como un sustituto o una cima que cubrir, sino por ofrecer justo por su naturaleza algo que los demás de más envergadura y renombre no pueden: la ligereza y la claridad de un juego sin abalorios, desnudo y auténtico.
